### Assistant / 20/05/2026 10:05:23.562

DataTrap’s README shows the biggest difference: it is dataset-first and multi-protocol, while FunnyPot is currently SSH-first with rich LLM telemetry and dashboarding. I’m checking their docs/source layout for specific implementation patterns worth borrowing.

### Assistant / 20/05/2026 10:06:04.223

**Quick Take**
Thales `dd-honeypot` is broader and more modular. FunnyPot is narrower but deeper: SSH-only, LLM-first, stronger behavioral analytics, and a better public dashboard.

The most useful inspiration is not “copy their whole architecture”; it is to borrow 3 ideas:

1. Dataset-first responses before LLM fallback.
2. Fake filesystem/state model for shell realism.
3. Config-driven personas/services instead of one hardcoded Omega-Black profile.

**Comparison**
| Area | FunnyPot | Thales DataTrap |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Protocols | SSH only | SSH, HTTP/S, MySQL, PostgreSQL, Redis, Telnet, generic TCP |
| Response model | LLM-first shell emulation | Dataset lookup first, LLM fallback second |
| Persona | Hardcoded Omega-Black prompt in `FunnyPot/Program.cs` | Config-driven `config.json` per honeypot |
| Shell realism | Prompt + LLM, some command validation | Prompt templates, session state, fake filesystem |
| Telemetry | Strong: auth, credentials, commands, MITRE-ish tactics, payload metadata, dashboard | Structured JSON logs, S3/CloudWatch/fluent-bit oriented |
| Dashboard | Strong static dashboard in `frontend-main/index.html` | Not dashboard-focused |
| Persistence | GitHub Pages/data branch | Cloud logging/data lake style |
| LLM rate control | Basic delay via `LLM_DELAY_MS` | Per-visitor LLM invoke limiter |
| Extensibility | Mostly one large `Program.cs` | Registry + base honeypot + protocol handlers + data handlers |

**Best Feature Ideas**
1. **Dataset-First Command Cache**
Add a small JSONL response dataset before calling the LLM.

Why:
- Makes common commands instant and consistent.
- Reduces OpenRouter cost/latency.
- Avoids LLM drift for commands like `uname -a`, `whoami`, `id`, `pwd`, `ls`, `cat /etc/os-release`.

How it would fit:
- Before `GetLLMResponse(...)` in `FunnyPot/Program.cs`, check `data/ssh_responses.jsonl`.
- If matched, return the static response.
- If unmatched, use LLM and optionally log it as a candidate dataset entry.

High value, low risk.

2. **Fake Filesystem Layer**
Borrow DataTrap’s fake FS concept, but keep it simple.

Current FunnyPot relies on the prompt to keep files consistent:
- `/home/secretOps/.env`
- `/root/.ssh/id_rsa`
- `/etc/shadow`
- `/home/secretOps/mission_brief.txt`

Better:
- Add a `fake_fs.json` with paths, file type, permissions, owner, contents.
- Intercept `ls`, `pwd`, `cd`, `cat`, `head`, `tail`, `stat`, `find`.
- Only use LLM for unknown/complex commands.

This would make attackers see a much more coherent machine.

3. **Config-Driven Persona**
Right now the persona is embedded in `BuildSystemPrompt(...)`.

Borrow Thales’ `config.json` style:
```json
{
  "name": "Omega-Black",
  "hostname": "omegablack",
  "os": "Debian GNU/Linux 12",
  "prompt_template": "{{username}}@omegablack>$ ",
  "system_prompt_file": "personas/omega-black.md",
  "fake_fs_file": "personas/omega-black.fs.json"
}
```

Benefits:
- You can run different personas without editing C#.
- Easier A/B tests: “Debian research server” vs “Alpine IoT box” vs “Kubernetes node”.
- Dashboard can compare engagement by persona.

4. **LLM Fallback Enrichment Queue**
DataTrap logs unknown requests for review/future inclusion.

FunnyPot could add:
- `frontend/data/llm_candidates.jsonl`
- Store command, LLM response, session context, whether response seemed successful
- Dashboard tab: “Candidate Responses”
- Button/manual workflow later: promote to static dataset

This turns live attacker traffic into a growing response library.

5. **Per-IP LLM Rate Limiting**
Thales has visitor-based invoke limiting.

FunnyPot should probably add:
- Max LLM calls per remote IP per time window
- Fallback to static “busy/permission/no output” responses after limit
- Dashboard metric: “LLM Limited IPs”

This protects cost and prevents one bot from burning tokens.

6. **SCP Upload Capture**
DataTrap’s SSH handler supports SCP upload capture.

FunnyPot currently blocks SCP/SFTP. That is safe, but less informative.

Alternative:
- Accept SCP upload into a quarantine directory.
- Hash file, size-limit it, never execute it.
- Log filename, size, SHA-256, remote IP, session.
- Show uploaded payloads on dashboard.

This is a strong honeypot feature if implemented carefully.

7. **Protocol Expansion: Redis First**
Do not jump to all 7 protocols. Start with Redis.

Why Redis:
- Simple protocol.
- Common internet scan target.
- Attackers often try `CONFIG SET dir`, `SLAVEOF`, `REPLICAOF`, `SET`, `GET`, `KEYS`.
- Easy to log high-signal commands.

A lightweight Redis honeypot next to SSH would add a lot of visibility without huge complexity.

8. **HTTP Trap Endpoint**
Add a minimal HTTP honeypot on `80`/`8080`.

Useful paths:
- `/`
- `/admin`
- `/login`
- `/phpmyadmin`
- `/wp-login.php`
- `/cgi-bin/luci/`
- `/.env`

This would complement SSH and feed the same dashboard with protocol dimension.

9. **Data Lake Export**
FunnyPot’s GitHub Pages flow is nice for public visibility. Thales’ S3/fluent-bit/Athena path is better for long-term analysis.

Possible middle ground:
- Keep GitHub Pages for summary.
- Add optional compressed archive export: `harvest-YYYY-MM-DD-HH.jsonl.gz`.
- Add `EXPORT_ARCHIVE_DIR` or S3-compatible env later.

10. **Dispatcher/Lateral Movement Simulation**
This is the most ambitious Thales idea: route post-compromise behavior to fake services.

FunnyPot version:
- If attacker runs `mysql -h 127.0.0.1`, enter a fake MySQL REPL.
- If attacker runs `redis-cli`, enter fake Redis mode.
- If attacker curls `localhost:8080`, return fake HTTP service content.

No need for real proxying initially. Just mode-switch inside the shell.

**Priority Recommendation**
1. Dataset-first command responses.
2. Fake filesystem for common file commands.
3. Config-driven persona.
4. Per-IP LLM rate limiting.
5. SCP upload capture.
6. Redis or HTTP mini-honeypot.
7. Candidate-response review tab.
8. Long-term archive export.
9. Shell mode-switching for fake internal services.

If we build only one next: **dataset-first responses + fake filesystem**. That gives the biggest realism/cost win while preserving FunnyPot’s current architecture.

### User / 20/05/2026 10:17:33.234

Create a detailed implementation plan for items 1,2,4,5

### Assistant / 20/05/2026 10:17:33.243

**Implementation Plan**

Target features:

1. Dataset-first command responses
2. Fake filesystem layer
4. Per-IP LLM rate limiting
5. SCP upload capture

Recommended order:

1. Dataset-first responses
2. Fake filesystem
3. Per-IP LLM rate limiting
4. SCP upload capture

Reason: dataset + fake FS reduce LLM usage and make behavior deterministic. Rate limiting then becomes less disruptive because common commands still work. SCP capture is separate and can be added after shell behavior is stable.

**1. Dataset-First Command Responses**

Goal: before calling the LLM, check a local static response dataset for known commands and return deterministic output.

Current hook point:
`FunnyPot/Program.cs`

Inside `ProcessLine(...)`, the current flow is:

```csharp
Logger.LogYaml("command", ...);
InputValidator.Validate(line);
SCPDetector.IsSCPCommand(line);
Thread.Sleep(LlmDelayMs);
var (response, promptTokens, completionTokens, _) = GetLLMResponse(messageHistory, line);
```

New flow:

```text
command received
validate/block dangerous input
try fake filesystem
try static dataset lookup
try LLM if no match and rate limit allows
fallback response if LLM unavailable/limited
log command_result
```

Files to add:

- `FunnyPot/data/ssh_responses.jsonl`
- Optional later: `FunnyPot/data/llm_candidates.jsonl`

Example `ssh_responses.jsonl`:

```jsonl
{"match":"exact","command":"whoami","response":"remote"}
{"match":"exact","command":"id","response":"uid=1001(remote) gid=1001(users) groups=1001(users)"}
{"match":"regex","command":"^uname\\s+-a$","response":"Linux omegablack 5.15.0-91-generic #101-Ubuntu SMP x86_64 GNU/Linux"}
{"match":"exact","command":"pwd","response":"/home/remote"}
{"match":"exact","command":"hostname","response":"omegablack"}
{"match":"exact","command":"cat /etc/os-release","response":"PRETTY_NAME=\"Debian GNU/Linux 12 (bookworm)\"\nNAME=\"Debian GNU/Linux\"\nVERSION_ID=\"12\"\nVERSION=\"12 (bookworm)\""}
```

Classes/helpers to add in `Program.cs` or a new file later:

```csharp
public class StaticCommandResponse
{
    public string Match { get; set; } = "exact";
    public string Command { get; set; } = "";
    public string Response { get; set; } = "";
}
```

Helper:

```csharp
static class StaticResponseStore
{
    static IReadOnlyList<StaticCommandResponse> LoadResponses();
    public static bool TryGetResponse(string command, out string response);
}
```

Minimal implementation details:

- Load JSONL lazily once.
- Support `exact` and `regex`.
- Normalize exact commands with `.Trim()`.
- Use case-sensitive matching for shell realism, except maybe command normalization around whitespace.
- Return empty string for commands with no output, e.g. `true`, `cd` once fake FS owns `cd`.
- If dataset file missing, silently skip.

Telemetry additions:

Add a source field to `CommandResultLogEntry`:

```csharp
public string ResponseSource { get; set; } = "llm";
```

Values:

- `fake_fs`
- `dataset`
- `llm`
- `rate_limited`
- `error_fallback`

Dashboard metrics later:

- Dataset hit ratio
- LLM avoided calls
- Top unmatched commands

Tests to add:

- Exact match returns expected response.
- Regex match returns expected response.
- Unknown command falls through.
- Missing dataset does not crash.

**2. Fake Filesystem Layer**

Goal: deterministic shell state and realistic output for common filesystem commands.

Current issue:
FunnyPot’s system prompt mentions fixed files, but the LLM can drift. Commands like `cd`, `pwd`, `ls`, `cat`, `find`, `stat`, `head`, `tail` should not need LLM.

Files to add:

- `FunnyPot/data/fake_fs.json`

Example:

```json
{
  "home": "/home/remote",
  "nodes": [
    {
      "path": "/",
      "type": "directory",
      "owner": "root",
      "group": "root",
      "mode": "755"
    },
    {
      "path": "/home/remote",
      "type": "directory",
      "owner": "remote",
      "group": "users",
      "mode": "755"
    },
    {
      "path": "/etc/os-release",
      "type": "file",
      "owner": "root",
      "group": "root",
      "mode": "644",
      "content": "PRETTY_NAME=\"Debian GNU/Linux 12 (bookworm)\"\nNAME=\"Debian GNU/Linux\"\nVERSION_ID=\"12\"\nVERSION=\"12 (bookworm)\"\n"
    },
    {
      "path": "/home/secretOps/.env",
      "type": "file",
      "owner": "secretOps",
      "group": "secretOps",
      "mode": "600",
      "content": "AWS_ACCESS_KEY_ID=AKIAIOSFODNN7EXAMPLE\nAWS_SECRET_ACCESS_KEY=wJalrXUtnFEMI/K7MDENG/bPxRfiCYEXAMPLEKEY\nDB_PASSWORD=s3cr3t!Vault99\n"
    }
  ]
}
```

New session state:

In the session model inside `SetupShell`, add:

```csharp
var currentWorkingDirectory = "/home/remote";
```

or better:

```csharp
var shellState = new ShellState
{
    CurrentDirectory = "/home/remote",
    Username = username
};
```

New classes:

```csharp
public class FakeFsConfig
{
    public string Home { get; set; } = "/home/remote";
    public List<FakeFsNode> Nodes { get; set; } = new();
}

public class FakeFsNode
{
    public string Path { get; set; } = "";
    public string Type { get; set; } = "file";
    public string Owner { get; set; } = "root";
    public string Group { get; set; } = "root";
    public string Mode { get; set; } = "644";
    public string Content { get; set; } = "";
}
```

```csharp
public class ShellState
{
    public string CurrentDirectory { get; set; } = "/home/remote";
    public string Username { get; set; } = "remote";
}
```

Helper:

```csharp
static class FakeFileSystem
{
    public static bool TryHandle(string command, ShellState state, out string response);
}
```

Commands to implement first:

- `pwd`
- `cd`
- `ls`
- `ls -la`
- `cat <path>`
- `head <path>`
- `tail <path>`
- `stat <path>`
- `find <path> -maxdepth N`
- `whoami`
- `hostname`

Path behavior:

- Support absolute paths.
- Support relative paths.
- Support `~`.
- Support `.` and `..`.
- Normalize multiple slashes.
- Prevent escaping root.

Permission behavior, simple version:

- If file mode starts with `6` and owner is not current user, return `Permission denied`.
- `/root/*` should deny for non-root.
- `/etc/shadow` should deny unless fake root mode is later added.
- `/home/secretOps/.env` may either deny or intentionally leak depending on honeypot persona. For realism, I’d make it readable only after a fake escalation path is implemented; for bait value, allow `cat` and mark it as high-value access.

Logging additions:

Add to `CommandLogEntry` or `CommandResultLogEntry`:

```csharp
public string? WorkingDirectory { get; set; }
public string ResponseSource { get; set; } = "";
```

Dashboard additions later:

- Top accessed files
- Sensitive file reads
- Permission-denied ratio
- Directory traversal behavior

Tests:

- `pwd` reflects initial home.
- `cd /tmp` changes state.
- `cd ..` normalizes correctly.
- `cat /etc/os-release` returns configured content.
- `cat /root/.ssh/id_rsa` returns permission denied.
- Unknown commands fall through to dataset/LLM.

**3. Per-IP LLM Rate Limiting**

Goal: stop one scanner/session from causing unlimited LLM calls, while still letting deterministic commands work.

Current behavior:
Every non-blocked, non-SCP command reaches `GetLLMResponse(...)`.

New behavior:

```text
fake FS match -> no LLM
dataset match -> no LLM
unknown command -> check rate limit
if allowed -> LLM
if denied -> realistic fallback response
```

Environment variables:

```text
LLM_MAX_CALLS_PER_IP=20
LLM_RATE_WINDOW_SECONDS=3600
LLM_RATE_LIMIT_RESPONSE=sh: command not found
```

Better fallback logic:

- If command looks like executable: `<cmd>: command not found`
- If command is `cat <unknown>`: `cat: <path>: No such file or directory`
- If command is `ls <unknown>`: `ls: cannot access '<path>': No such file or directory`
- Else: empty output or `bash: <cmd>: command not found`

New helper:

```csharp
static class LlmRateLimiter
{
    public static bool TryConsume(string remoteEndpoint, out int remaining, out DateTime resetAt);
}
```

State:

```csharp
static readonly ConcurrentDictionary<string, LlmRateBucket> LlmBuckets = new(StringComparer.OrdinalIgnoreCase);
```

Bucket:

```csharp
public class LlmRateBucket
{
    public DateTime WindowStartedAt { get; set; }
    public int Count { get; set; }
}
```

Keying:

Use existing:

```csharp
Program.GetRemoteAttemptKey(remoteEndpoint)
```

Important design point:

Rate limiting should happen after fake FS and dataset lookup. Otherwise common commands become artificially broken.

Telemetry additions:

Add event:

```csharp
Logger.LogYaml("llm_rate_limited", new LlmRateLimitLogEntry { ... });
```

Class:

```csharp
public class LlmRateLimitLogEntry
{
    public DateTime Timestamp { get; set; }
    public string SessionKey { get; set; } = "";
    public string ShellSessionId { get; set; } = "";
    public string RemoteEndpoint { get; set; } = "";
    public string RemoteIp { get; set; } = "";
    public string Command { get; set; } = "";
    public int Limit { get; set; }
    public DateTime ResetAt { get; set; }
}
```

Add summary fields:

```csharp
public int TotalLlmCalls { get; set; }
public int TotalLlmRateLimited { get; set; }
public Dictionary<string, int> LlmCallsByIp { get; set; } = new();
public Dictionary<string, int> LlmRateLimitedByIp { get; set; } = new();
```

Dashboard additions:

- LLM calls
- LLM limited commands
- LLM avoided by dataset/fake FS
- Top limited IPs

Tests:

- Limit allows first N calls.
- N+1 call is rejected.
- Window resets after configured period.
- Different IPs have separate buckets.
- Dataset/fake FS calls do not consume LLM budget.

**4. SCP Upload Capture**

Goal: safely capture attempted uploads without executing anything.

Current behavior:
`SCPDetector.IsSCPCommand(line)` blocks SCP/SFTP commands inside interactive shell.

But real SCP upload usually comes through SSH `exec` channel, not interactive shell. FxSsh may expose this differently from Paramiko, so implementation should be staged.

Stage A: improve interactive detection/logging.

When command matches SCP/SFTP:

- Log `scp_attempt`
- Keep blocking interactive SCP as today
- Capture metadata: command, direction, target path, remote endpoint

Class:

```csharp
public class ScpAttemptLogEntry
{
    public DateTime Timestamp { get; set; }
    public string SessionKey { get; set; } = "";
    public string ShellSessionId { get; set; } = "";
    public string RemoteEndpoint { get; set; } = "";
    public string Username { get; set; } = "";
    public string Command { get; set; } = "";
    public string Direction { get; set; } = "unknown";
    public string TargetPath { get; set; } = "";
    public string Status { get; set; } = "blocked";
}
```

Stage B: add exec-channel handling if FxSsh supports it.

Need to inspect FxSsh `CommandOpened`/channel behavior further. Current code only handles:

```csharp
if (args.ShellType != "shell") return;
```

Plan:

```csharp
conn.CommandOpened += (_, args) =>
{
    if (args.ShellType == "shell")
        HandleInteractiveShell(...);

    if (args.ShellType == "exec")
        HandleExecCommand(...);
};
```

If FxSsh exposes command string:

- Detect `scp -t <path>` for upload.
- Send SCP protocol ACK byte `\0`.
- Read file header: `C0644 <size> <filename>\n`.
- Enforce size limit.
- Read exactly `<size>` bytes.
- Hash with SHA-256.
- Save to quarantine.
- Send final ACK.
- Close channel.

If FxSsh does not expose enough exec-channel primitives:

- Keep Stage A.
- Log unsupported SCP exec attempts if visible.
- Consider adding a small integration test with real `scp -P 22722 file test@host:/tmp/`.

Config:

```text
SCP_CAPTURE_ENABLED=true
SCP_CAPTURE_DIR=/var/log/funnypot/uploads
SCP_MAX_UPLOAD_BYTES=10485760
```

Quarantine path:

```text
/var/log/funnypot/uploads/<yyyyMMdd>/<sessionId>/<sha256>_<safeFilename>
```

Security constraints:

- Never execute uploaded files.
- Never preserve attacker-supplied paths directly.
- Sanitize filename with basename only.
- Enforce max upload size.
- Timeout reads.
- Store with restrictive permissions if possible.
- Hash while streaming to avoid large memory use.

Upload log class:

```csharp
public class ScpUploadLogEntry
{
    public DateTime Timestamp { get; set; }
    public string SessionKey { get; set; } = "";
    public string ShellSessionId { get; set; } = "";
    public string RemoteEndpoint { get; set; } = "";
    public string Username { get; set; } = "";
    public string Filename { get; set; } = "";
    public string TargetPath { get; set; } = "";
    public long BytesReceived { get; set; }
    public string Sha256 { get; set; } = "";
    public string StoredPath { get; set; } = "";
    public string Status { get; set; } = "";
    public string Error { get; set; } = "";
}
```

Dashboard additions:

- SCP attempts
- Uploaded payloads
- Top filenames
- SHA-256 list
- Upload sizes
- Upload status

Tests:

- SCP command parser identifies upload mode.
- Filename sanitization removes paths.
- Oversized upload is rejected.
- Hash is computed correctly.
- Failed upload logs status and does not crash session.

**Refactoring Needed**

Before implementing all four, make one small structural improvement:

Extract shell command execution into one helper inside `SetupShell` or nearby:

```csharp
CommandExecutionResult ResolveCommand(
    string line,
    ShellState state,
    string remoteEndpoint,
    string sessionKey,
    string shellSessionId)
```

Result:

```csharp
public class CommandExecutionResult
{
    public string Response { get; set; } = "";
    public string Source { get; set; } = "";
    public int PromptTokens { get; set; }
    public int CompletionTokens { get; set; }
    public bool UsedLlm { get; set; }
    public bool RateLimited { get; set; }
}
```

Resolution order:

```text
1. Fake filesystem
2. Static dataset
3. LLM rate limiter
4. LLM
5. Fallback
```

This keeps `ProcessLine(...)` readable and avoids piling more logic into the already-large method.

**Implementation Milestones**

Milestone 1: Dataset-first

- Add `ssh_responses.jsonl`
- Add loader + matcher
- Add `ResponseSource`
- Wire before LLM
- Add tests

Milestone 2: Fake FS

- Add `fake_fs.json`
- Add shell state
- Implement path normalization
- Implement `pwd`, `cd`, `ls`, `cat`
- Wire before dataset
- Add tests

Milestone 3: Rate limiting

- Add env vars
- Add limiter
- Add `llm_rate_limited` event
- Add summary counters
- Add dashboard metrics
- Add tests

Milestone 4: SCP capture

- Add SCP attempt logging for current interactive block path
- Investigate FxSsh exec command support
- If supported, implement upload protocol
- Add quarantine storage
- Add dashboard payload/upload panel
- Add tests/integration test with `scp`

**Risks**

- FxSsh may not expose enough SCP exec-channel detail for true upload capture.
- Fake FS can become complex if trying to emulate full Bash semantics. Keep first version command-specific.
- Static dataset matching can accidentally return unrealistic output for commands with flags. Start conservative.
- Rate limiting can reduce engagement if fallback responses are too obviously fake. Put limiter after fake FS/dataset to minimize that.

## Goal
- Maintain and enhance the FunnyPot SSH honeypot dashboard, data pipeline, and deployment automation

## Constraints & Preferences
- Dashboard should show credential stats, geography, activity; avoid cramped single-page UI
- Data branch should retain recent hours (12h) after pruning test data
- Deployments must handle submodule commits cleanly and avoid push races with live container

## Progress
### Done
- Added tooltips with question mark icons to all dashboard metrics for explanations on hover
- Added `Unique Scan IPs` and `Shell Opens` metrics to `harvest_summary.json`
- Integrated `FunnyPot.ai` as submodules: `frontend` (data branch) and `frontend-main` (dashboard)
- Fixed Git publication to fetch `origin/data` at startup, avoiding non-fast-forward rejections
- Added credential frequency (top usernames/passwords) to summary and dashboard
- Pruned data branch to latest 12 hours, removing early test telemetry
- Created `scripts/deploy.sh` that updates graphify, commits/pushes submodules and parent, rebuilds Docker
- Added tabbed dashboard layout (Overview, Credentials, Geography, Activity)
- Added IP geolocation with dual providers (ipwho.is, ipinfo.io) and localStorage caching
- Added Organization/ASN distribution to Geography tab
- Fixed misleading "Mean Engagement" label to "Mean Connection"

### In Progress
- (none)

### Blocked
- (none)

## Key Decisions
- Used client-side IP geolocation with fallback providers to avoid backend changes
- Pruned data on live container to avoid push races; stopped container, pruned, pushed, restarted
- Deploy script checks for local submodule changes before pushing to avoid stale remote races
- Dashboard shows all retained historical data, not rolling window, after pruning test period

## Next Steps
- (none)

## Critical Context
- Data branch push races: container pushes new telemetry while we try to prune/push; solved by pausing container
- Submodule detection bug: `.git` is a gitfile not a directory; fixed by checking `-e` instead of `-d`
- IP geolocation returns "Unknown" if browser blocks requests or APIs fail; added dual provider fallback
- Comparison with Thales dd-honeypot revealed opportunities for multi-protocol support, dispatcher architecture, and dataset-driven responses

## Relevant Files
- `FunnyPot/Program.cs`: backend logic for auth, harvest, Git publishing, LLM responses, DataHarvester
- `frontend-main/index.html`: dashboard UI with tabs, geography, credential stats, tooltips
- `frontend/data/harvest_summary.json`: retained telemetry aggregates
- `scripts/deploy.sh`: automated deploy helper used for every push/redeploy

### User / 20/05/2026 13:38:18.202

Software Requirements Specification (SRS)

FunnyPot SSH Honeypot Enhancements

Version: 1.0
Date: 2026-05-20

1. Introduction

1.1 Purpose

This document defines the software requirements for extending the FunnyPot SSH honeypot platform with deterministic command handling, virtual filesystem emulation, per-IP LLM rate limiting, and SCP upload capture capabilities.

The purpose of these enhancements is to:

Reduce unnecessary LLM utilization

Improve shell realism and consistency

Improve attacker engagement quality

Enhance telemetry collection

Safely capture attacker-uploaded payloads

Improve operational stability and deployment predictability


1.2 Scope

The project extends the existing FunnyPot SSH honeypot backend, dashboard telemetry, and deployment workflows.

The implementation introduces:

1. Dataset-first deterministic command responses


2. Virtual filesystem emulation layer


3. Per-IP LLM request rate limiting


4. SCP upload attempt and payload capture


5. Refactored shell command resolution pipeline



The system shall continue supporting:

Existing SSH shell interaction workflows

Existing telemetry collection

Existing Git publication pipeline

Existing dashboard functionality

Existing deployment automation


1.3 Definitions and Acronyms

Term Description

LLM Large Language Model
SCP Secure Copy Protocol
SSH Secure Shell
JSONL JSON Lines format
Fake FS Virtualized filesystem emulation layer
Telemetry Operational and behavioral logging
FxSsh SSH library currently used by FunnyPot
Session Key Unique identifier for remote interaction grouping


2. Overall Description

2.1 Product Perspective

FunnyPot is an SSH honeypot platform that currently relies heavily on LLM-generated responses for shell interaction.

The new architecture introduces a layered command resolution pipeline:

1. Fake filesystem resolution


2. Static dataset lookup


3. LLM rate limit validation


4. LLM execution


5. Fallback response generation



This architecture shifts common shell behavior away from probabilistic LLM generation toward deterministic simulation.

2.2 Product Functions

The enhanced system shall provide:

Deterministic shell responses for common commands

Persistent session shell state

Filesystem navigation emulation

Realistic permission handling

Per-IP LLM throttling

SCP upload detection and capture

Enhanced telemetry and analytics

Extended dashboard metrics


2.3 User Classes

User Class Description

Security Researcher Reviews attacker telemetry and uploaded payloads
SOC Analyst Uses dashboard metrics and behavioral analytics
Platform Administrator Maintains deployments and data pipelines
Threat Hunter Reviews captured attacker TTPs and filesystem interactions


2.4 Assumptions and Dependencies

Assumptions

SSH interactions remain text-shell oriented

Most attacker interactions use common shell commands

Fake filesystem realism is sufficient without full Bash emulation

Static dataset responses improve engagement quality


Dependencies

.NET runtime

FxSsh library

Existing FunnyPot logging framework

Existing YAML telemetry pipeline

Existing Docker deployment workflow


3. System Architecture

3.1 High-Level Architecture

The command resolution flow shall be:

SSH Command
    ↓
Input Validation
    ↓
SCP Detection
    ↓
Fake Filesystem Handler
    ↓
Static Dataset Lookup
    ↓
LLM Rate Limiter
    ↓
LLM Response Generation
    ↓
Fallback Generator
    ↓
Telemetry Logging

3.2 Core Components

Component Responsibility

FakeFileSystem Deterministic shell filesystem emulation
StaticResponseStore Static dataset response handling
LlmRateLimiter Per-IP LLM quota enforcement
SCPDetector SCP/SFTP command detection
SCPUploadHandler SCP upload stream capture
CommandResolver Unified command resolution pipeline
Dashboard Aggregator Metric generation and aggregation


4. Functional Requirements

4.1 Dataset-First Command Responses

4.1.1 Overview

The system shall resolve known shell commands from a static dataset before invoking the LLM.

4.1.2 Data Source

### User / 20/05/2026 13:55:30.746

Match Type Description
exact Exact command string match
regex Regular expression match
4.1.4 Functional Requirements
FR-DS-001
The system shall lazily load the dataset on first access.

FR-DS-002
The system shall tolerate missing dataset files without terminating execution.

FR-DS-003
The system shall normalize exact-match commands using Trim().

FR-DS-004
The system shall support case-sensitive matching.

FR-DS-005
The system shall return deterministic responses without invoking the LLM when a dataset match exists.

FR-DS-006
The system shall log the response source as "dataset".

FR-DS-007
The system shall support empty responses for commands with no output.

Deterministic commands bypass LLM usage

Per-IP rate limiting functions correctly

<invoke name="edit">
<parameter name="filePath">/home/felix/FunnyPot/FunnyPot/Program.cs</parameter>
<parameter name="oldString">static class SCPDetector
{
    private static readonly Regex ScpUploadPattern = new(@"(?i)^\s*scp\s+.*(-t|-r|-p)\s+", RegexOptions.Compiled);
    private static readonly Regex ScpDownloadPattern = new(@"(?i)^\s*scp\s+.*(-f)\s+", RegexOptions.Compiled);
    private static readonly Regex SftpPattern = new(@"(?i)^\s*(sftp|ssh|sftp\-server)", RegexOptions.Compiled);

    public static bool IsSCPCommand(string input)
    {
        return ScpUploadPattern.IsMatch(input) ||
               ScpDownloadPattern.IsMatch(input) ||
               SftpPattern.IsMatch(input);
    }

    public static bool IsSCPUpload(string input)
    {
        return ScpUploadPattern.IsMatch(input);
    }

    public static (string? remotePath, string? localPath) ParseSCPUpload(string input)
    {
        var match = Regex.Match(input, @"(?i)scp\s+(?:-[tprdD]+\s+)?(\S+)\s+(\S+)$");
        if (match.Success)
        {
            var localPath = match.Groups[2].Value;
            return (localPath, localPath);
        }
        return (null, null);
    }
}</parameter>
<parameter name="newString">static class SCPDetector
{
    private static readonly Regex ScpUploadPattern = new(@"(?i)^\s*scp\s+.*(-t|-r|-p)\s+", RegexOptions.Compiled);
    private static readonly Regex ScpDownloadPattern = new(@"(?i)^\s*scp\s+.*(-f)\s+", RegexOptions.Compiled);
    private static readonly Regex SftpPattern = new(@"(?i)^\s*(sftp|ssh|sftp\-server)", RegexOptions.Compiled);
    private static readonly Regex SimpleScpPattern = new(@"^(?i)scp(\s|$)", RegexOptions.Compiled);

    public static bool IsSCPCommand(string input)
    {
        return SimpleScpPattern.IsMatch(input) ||
               ScpUploadPattern.IsMatch(input) ||
               ScpDownloadPattern.IsMatch(input) ||
               SftpPattern.IsMatch(input);
    }

    public static bool IsSCPUpload(string input)
    {
        return ScpUploadPattern.IsMatch(input);
    }

    public static (string? remotePath, string? localPath) ParseSCPUpload(string input)
    {
        var match = Regex.Match(input, @"(?i)scp\s+(?:-[tprdD]+\s+)?(\S+)\s+(\S+)$");
        if (match.Success)
        {
            var localPath = match.Groups[2].Value;
            return (localPath, localPath);
        }
        return (null, null);
    }
}</parameter>
</invoke>

### User / 20/05/2026 14:11:31.611

Question : does an attacker knows what type of endpoint he is scanning before ssh authorization stage?

### Assistant / 20/05/2026 14:11:31.621

Yes. Before SSH authorization (key exchange), the attacker sees the **SSH banner** (e.g., `SSH-2.0-OmegaBlack_Classified_Server_v1.0`) and the **TCP port** is known from the scan itself. Tools like `nmap` fingerprint the SSH implementation from the banner and key exchange response to identify the server software before any auth attempt.

### User / 20/05/2026 14:13:26.617

### Assistant / 20/05/2026 14:13:26.628

Set via `SSH_BANNER` env var, defaults to that in `FunnyPot/Program.cs:27`.

### User / 20/05/2026 15:30:16.981

What are the typical SSH burners for cameras and plcs

### Assistant / 20/05/2026 15:30:16.991

Common SSH banners for cameras and PLCs:

**Cameras (IP cameras, NVRs):**
- `SSH-2.0-dropbear` — most common (BusyBox/Dropbear embedded)
- `SSH-2.0-OpenSSH_6.6.1` — older Hikvision, Dahua
- `SSH-2.0-OpenSSH_7.4` — newer Hikvision

**PLCs / Industrial:**
- `SSH-2.0-OpenSSH_6.0p1 Debian` — Siemens S7-1500
- `SSH-2.0-dropbear_2019.78` — Allen-Bradley CompactLogix
- `SSH-2.0-OpenSSH_7.9` — Rockwell Automation
- `SSH-2.0-OpenSSH_for_Windows_8.1` — older SCADA servers

**Generic embedded (both often share):**
- `SSH-2.0-dropbear_2020.80`
- `SSH-2.0-OpenSSH_7.2p2 Ubuntu`

Want me to change the FunnyPot banner to one of these for better camouflage?

### User / 20/05/2026 15:53:30.528

I would prefer the banners and automatically switching between each new session and it can be locked with with the session information so we can later make a statistics pair this better type do not make several ports simultaneously

### Assistant / 20/05/2026 15:53:30.542

That's technically not possible with FxSsh — the SSH banner is sent during TCP handshake, before `ConnectionAccepted` fires. By the time our code runs, the banner has already been transmitted. The library has no pre-handshake hook to select a different banner per connection on a single port.

The current multi-port approach is the only way to serve different banners, and it actually gives us **perfect** session-to-banner locking for accurate statistics (each session is attributed to the exact banner on the port it hit).

### Assistant / 20/05/2026 18:14:06.948

I see the issue. The Docker image was built with the old `frontend/` submodule state. ### User / 21/05/2026 09:10:56.771

I have hundreds of connections, but not even one shell... I suspect the shell emulation doesn't work

### Assistant / 21/05/2026 09:10:56.776

I’ll inspect the project map first, then trace where connection handling and shell emulation are implemented so I can verify the suspected failure path.

### Assistant / 21/05/2026 09:11:07.407

The graph points to `Program` as the SSH/session hub, and the shell code is concentrated in `FunnyPot/Program.cs`. I’m going to inspect that path and verify against the FxSsh API behavior.

### Assistant / 21/05/2026 09:11:39.278

The server accepts auth after the configured/password-harvest threshold, then relies on FxSsh `CommandOpened` with `ShellType == "shell"` to start the emulation. I’m going to reproduce with a local SSH client to see whether that event fires.

### Assistant / 21/05/2026 09:14:14.702

Local reproduction shows interactive SSH does get a prompt and processes commands, so the shell path itself works. The gap is that `ssh host command` opens an `exec` request, and this server currently ignores every `CommandOpened` that is not exactly `shell`, so many real attackers/bots can authenticate and run no emulated command. I’m going to make `exec` requests use the same command resolver and close after the one command.

### Assistant / 21/05/2026 09:33:03.285

Implemented a fix in `FunnyPot/Program.cs`.

Root cause: interactive shells worked, but `exec` requests were ignored:

```csharp
if (args.ShellType != "shell") return;
```

Many bots use `ssh user@host command`, which opens `exec`, not `shell`, so they authenticated but never entered emulation.

Changed behavior:
- Handles both `shell` and `exec`.
- Interactive shell still gets prompts.
- `exec` runs the command through the same resolver and closes after output.
- Logs `ExecOpened` for exec sessions.
- SCP/SFTP exec attempts now get blocked and closed.

Verified:
- `dotnet build FunnyPot/FunnyPot.csproj` passes.
- `dotnet test` passes: 39/39.
- Local SSH smoke test:
  - `ssh ... whoami` returns `remote`.
  - Interactive `ssh -tt ...` still gets prompt and works.

Also ran `graphify update .` as required; it updated `graphify-out/*`.

### User / 22/05/2026 19:08:42.216

הפקודות העיקריות שהריצו אצלי ב הFunnyPot. תסביר למה...

### Assistant / 22/05/2026 19:08:42.260

אלו בעיקר פקודות **fingerprinting / reconnaissance**. כלומר, התוקפים או הסורקים מנסים להבין מהר “לאן הם נפלו”: איזו מערכת הפעלה, איזה קרנל, איזה hostname, האם זו מכונה אמיתית, ומה אפשר להריץ עליה.

`uname -s -v -n -r -m`  
זו הפקודה הכי נפוצה אצלך בפער גדול. היא מחזירה:

```bash
-s  kernel name
-v  kernel version
-n  hostname
-r  kernel release
-m  machine architecture
```

למה מריצים אותה:  
כדי לזהות אם השרת הוא Linux, איזו גרסת kernel, מה הארכיטקטורה (`x86_64`, `arm`, וכו׳), ומה שם המכונה. זה עוזר לבוטים לבחור payload מתאים או להבין אם היעד מעניין.

`/bin/./uname -s -v -n -r -m`  
זו אותה פקודה, אבל עם נתיב “מוזר”:

```bash
/bin/./uname
```

למה ככה:  
בוטים עושים את זה כדי לעקוף aliases, wrappers, hooks, או סביבות מוגבלות שמנטרות `uname` רגיל. זה גם סימן לאוטומציה ולא למשתמש אנושי.

`uptime -p`  
מחזירה כמה זמן המערכת רצה בצורה קריאה.

למה מריצים אותה:  
כדי לבדוק אם המכונה נראית “אמיתית”, יציבה, ולא container/honeypot טרי. שרת עם uptime ארוך נתפס כיעד יותר אמין או מעניין.

`uname -s -m`  
גרסה מקוצרת של fingerprinting: מערכת הפעלה + ארכיטקטורה.

למה מריצים אותה:  
בחירה מהירה של binary/payload מתאים. למשל payload שונה ל־`x86_64` לעומת `arm`.

`46.117.25.20 ssh-rsa AAAA...`  
זה נראה כמו שורת SSH public key / host key ולא ממש פקודת shell רגילה.

למה זה מופיע:  
כנראה לקוח או סורק שלח מידע בינארי/פרוטוקולי לתוך shell או שה־honeypot תיעד ניסיון SSH חריג כפקודה. זה לא “פקודה” שימושית, אלא ארטיפקט של סריקה/אוטומציה.

`cat.gnupg[D[D[D...`  
זה נראה כמו ניסיון אנושי או כלי ששלח מקשי חצים/escape sequences.

למה זה מופיע:  
מישהו ניסה כנראה להקליד משהו כמו:

```bash
cat .gnupg
```

אבל התיעוד קלט גם תווי מקלדת כמו חץ שמאלה (`[D`). זה יותר אינטראקטיבי ופחות בוט נקי.

`cd Downloads`  
פקודת ניווט רגילה.

למה מריצים אותה:  
תוקף מחפש מקום להוריד payload, לבדוק קבצים, או להתנהג כמו משתמש רגיל. `Downloads` הוא יעד טבעי.

`echo 1 > /dev/null && cat /bin/echo`  
בדיקת shell/מערכת.

למה מריצים אותה:  
החלק הראשון בודק כתיבה/הרצת shell בלי להשאיר output. החלק השני קורא binary (`/bin/echo`) ויכול לשמש לבדוק הרשאות, קיום קבצים, או איך השרת מגיב לפלט בינארי.

`ls`  
רשימת קבצים.

למה מריצים אותה:  
בדיקת סביבת העבודה. זו אחת הפקודות הראשונות שתוקף מריץ אחרי כניסה כדי לראות איפה הוא נמצא ומה יש שם.

השורה התחתונה: רוב הפעילות אצלך נראית כמו **בוטים וסורקי SSH אוטומטיים** שמבצעים fingerprinting בסיסי אחרי login מוצלח. הכמות הגבוהה של `uname` מצביעה על תהליך אוטומטי שבודק במהירות מערכת, גרסה וארכיטקטורה לפני החלטה אם להמשיך להוריד payload או לעבור ליעד הבא.

### User / 22/05/2026 23:07:19.060

Review the code and suggest top 5 improvements in coding quality and top 5 in functionality

### User / 22/05/2026 23:09:03.879

Review the code and suggest top 5 improvements in coding quality and top 5 in functionality

### Assistant / 22/05/2026 23:09:58.938

Based on my review of the FunnyPot codebase, here are the top 5 coding quality improvements and top 5 functional improvements:

## Top 5 Coding Quality Improvements:

1. **Replace Magic Numbers with Named Constants**: Throughout the code, values like port numbers (22422), timeouts (300, 500), buffer sizes (4096, 100), and retry limits are hardcoded. These should be defined as named constants or configuration values for better maintainability.

2. **Improve Error Handling Consistency**: Many try-catch blocks silently swallow exceptions (e.g., in Logger.LogYaml, Logger.PushToGit, and various static constructors). This makes debugging difficult. Either log the exceptions or rethrow them appropriately.

3. **Reduce Code Duplication**: There's significant duplication in logging methods (LogMsg, LogYaml, LogMetric) and similar functionality across classes. These could be consolidated into a more unified logging approach.

4. **Enhance Thread Safety Review**: While ConcurrentDictionary is used appropriately in many places, some static classes have shared state that may benefit from additional thread safety review, particularly around shared resources like dictionaries and counters.

5. **Reduce Tight Coupling**: The code has tight coupling between components (e.g., static dependencies on Program class throughout). This reduces testability and flexibility. Consider dependency injection patterns or interfaces to decouple components.

## Top 5 Functional Improvements:

1. **Robust Configuration System**: Replace heavy reliance on environment variables with a proper configuration management system that supports validation, default values, and different configuration sources (files, environment variables, etc.).

2. **Enhanced Security Measures**: While the project has security-focused functionality, additional measures could include:
   - Improved input sanitization beyond current validation
   - More sophisticated rate limiting and anomaly detection
   - Secure storage for harvested credentials
   - Regular security audits of the codebase

3. **Increased Test Coverage**: The codebase would benefit from comprehensive unit and integration tests, particularly for:
   - Edge cases in command parsing and validation
   - Data harvesting and analysis functions
   - Network communication and error handling
   - Logging and metrics collection

4. **Performance Optimization**: Optimize high-frequency paths such as:
   - Command resolution and LLM interaction paths
   - Logging operations (which occur frequently)
   - Data structures used in hot paths (consider object pooling where appropriate)

5. **Improved Monitoring and Observability**: Add more comprehensive:
   - Health checks and diagnostics endpoints
   - Distributed tracing capabilities
   - Detailed metrics collection (beyond current basic stats)
   - Alerting mechanisms for anomalous behavior

These improvements would enhance both the maintainability of the codebase and its effectiveness as a security research tool.

### User / 23/05/2026 08:31:50.935

In the interactive sessions there are some entries with auth rejected status, explain

### Assistant / 23/05/2026 08:32:00.385

Great question — this is expected with your current auth flow.

Those `auth rejected` entries are per-attempt events, not “final session result” events.

Why you see them:

- Every auth try is logged as `auth_attempt` with `Accepted = true/false` in `FunnyPot/Program.cs:319`.
- Password auth starts as rejected unless:
  - credentials match configured `SSH_USER`/`SSH_PASSWORD`, or
  - the attempt count reaches the harvest threshold (`harvest_threshold`) in `FunnyPot/Program.cs:305`.
- Non-password methods are always forced to reject (`args.Result = false`) in `FunnyPot/Program.cs:339`.

So in one interactive connection, you can see:
1) one or more rejected attempts, then  
2) a later accepted attempt, then  
3) shell open (`shell_session_start`).

That’s why interactive sessions can still have some `auth rejected` records — those are earlier attempts in the same auth negotiation.

Also note:
- Attempt counter is keyed by remote IP (`remoteAttemptKey`) via `AuthAttempts.AddOrUpdate(...)` in `FunnyPot/Program.cs:292`, so behavior can carry across reconnects from the same source IP.

### User / 23/05/2026 08:34:59.864

I see that the majority of clients are SSH-2.0-Go, can I find a way to execute command on their side when they connect?

### Assistant / 23/05/2026 08:34:59.897

Short answer: no legitimate/safe way through normal SSH handshake — and I can’t help with exploiting clients.

What you can do safely instead:

- Treat `SSH-2.0-Go` as client fingerprinting only (often Go-based scanners/bots).
- Enrich logs per `ClientVersion` + source IP/ASN + command sequence timing.
- Add rules/labels in your analytics for `SSH-2.0-Go` cohorts (scanner vs interactive).
- Use deception content in shell responses to observe attacker behavior (no payloads).
- Correlate repeat sources and auto-block/tarpit at network layer (`iptables`/fail2ban).

If you want, I can help you add a “Go-client profile” report in your existing logs/dashboard (top IPs, auth pattern, first commands, session duration, automation score).

### User / 23/05/2026 09:16:04.070

What are the options to intrude or abuse or get extra info on attackers that connect to my ssh honeypot?

### Assistant / 23/05/2026 09:16:04.294

I won’t suggest actual exploitation techniques. Here are safe, useful ways you can gather more intel about your SSH clients:

- IP fingerprinting and geolocation:
  - Use MaxMind GeoIP or similar via mmdb tools; log country/city, ASN, ISP for each connection event.
  - Correlate with ASNs that frequently host scanners (e.g., DigitalOcean, AWS) to flag likely botnets.

- SSH version identification:
  - Log client and server versions from banners. Go clients often use ssh2.0; many known tools (GoSSH, libssh) show up there too.

- Connection patterns:
  - Track source IP/subnet, count of failed logins per minute, number of concurrent connections per user/source.
  - Spike in auth failures + specific client versions → high automation probability.

- Command pattern analysis:
  - Log first 10–20 commands sent after successful login (or even attempts).
  - Look for characteristic sequences from scanners, bots, or custom tools (e.t. ssh -T, curl/wget patterns in shell, etc.).

- Username / auth method behavior:
  - Monitor username trends per source; many random usernames + password failures → typical scanner pattern.
  - Watch for unusual auth methods combined with short session duration and no key reuse.

If you like, I can show how to add GeoIP/ASN logging or simple anomaly detection around these metrics in your current code (no invasive changes).

I won’t suggest any actual exploits or attack vectors—that would be risky and out of scope.

You can safely:

- Log more client context from SSH banners/env vars to improve detection.
- Track patterns like source IP, ASN, command sequences, timing anomalies, username rotation for better attribution without modifying attacker environments.
- Use existing tools (e.g., GeoIP lookups, simple rate limiting) at your edge or via firewall/fail2ban rather than inside the application code.

### User / 23/05/2026 09:26:52.642

If I know it's go implementation, can I use known vulnerabilities in the handshake stage?

### Assistant / 23/05/2026 09:26:52.679

Short answer: yes, there are historically relevant but mostly fixed or very difficult-to-exploit issues with Go’s SSH library that you could monitor for.

More useful and practical is how to detect them via your code without blindly trusting any one client. Some common patterns worth adding detection for:

1) Implement basic SSH version fingerprinting (for logging only):
   - Capture the server banner, so when they connect, log “SSH_20_Go” vs openSSH vs others. This helps distinguish known scanners/bots from humans you know your users are supposed to be using different clients.

2) Monitor for common Go SSH client fingerprints:
   - Example signatures (just as patterns, no RCEs):
     - SSH version string differences
     - Cipher list order or extension support behavior
     - Timing characteristics of key exchange and authentication requests
   You can add lightweight detectors in your auth/handshake flow that log a “likely_go” flag.

3) Add basic rate limiting / anomaly detection:
   - Limit concurrent connections from the same source IP.
   - Track failed login rates per IP, username; block or challenge after threshold.
   - Alert on: multiple usernames with high failure counts within short windows (classic automated credential stuffing pattern).

4) Inspect first few commands and environment variables:
   - Log env vars like SSH_CLIENT_VERSION, TERM=vt100 vs others for more fingerprinting data.
   - Record the first 5–20 commands after authentication; flag suspicious patterns:
     - Repeated short connections + random usernames + quick exit.
     - Common tool sequences (e.g., curl/wget pipes, one-liner tools).

5) Tighten SSH config at the system level instead of in your app code:
   - Allow only known users/keys or a limited set of auth methods via sshd_config where possible.
   - Use MaxStartups, PermitRootLogin no, PasswordAuthentication yes (or with MFA), etc., to limit attack surface.

If you’d like, I can help you add structured logging for these fingerprinting and rate-limiting signals into your existing code.

what would be an effort to add a web/http honeypot capability to this

### Assistant / 23/05/2026 09:36:07.987

To add a **Web/HTTP Honeypot** capability to **FunnyPot** alongside its existing SSH honeypot features, we would design and integrate an HTTP-based ingestion and simulation layer. 

Here is a comprehensive, low-impact architectural and implementation plan.

---

### 1. Architectural Strategy

We will run the HTTP honeypot concurrently with the existing SSH honeypot.
1. **Host Integration:** The HTTP service will bind to standard ports (e.g., `80` and `443` or a configurable port like `8080` mapped via Docker) alongside the FxSsh server.
2. **Telemetry Ingestion:** Any HTTP request (headers, paths, query params, body) will be parsed and routed through the same **Data Harvesting Subsystem (DHS)** pipeline to maintain unified dashboards and JSONL logging.
3. **Simulation Level:** 
   - **Static Web / Mock Service:** Return realistic mock HTTP responses (e.g., mock IIS, Apache, Nginx default pages, or common IoT login screens like TP-Link, admin panels, or phpMyAdmin).
   - **LLM-Driven Dynamic HTTP Simulation:** Route suspicious request paths (such as API probes, POST requests, or command injections) to the LLM backend. The LLM would formulate plausible HTTP headers, status codes, and response bodies, mimicking a vulnerable application (e.g., a vulnerable Spring Boot app or a Router Admin panel) to maximize attacker engagement time.

---

### 2. Required Code Modifications & Additions

#### **A. Configuration Upgrades (`AppConfiguration.cs` & `.env`)**
We will introduce configuration fields to control the HTTP server:
```csharp
public class HttpConfiguration
{
    public bool Enabled { get; set; } = true;
    public int Port { get; set; } = 8080;
    public string DefaultServerHeader { get; set; } = "nginx/1.24.0";
    public string DefaultResponseTemplate { get; set; } = "index.html"; // Mock index
}
```

#### **B. The HTTP Listener Layer (`HttpHoneypot.cs` - New Class)**
Using .NET’s built-in, lightweight `HttpListener` (or a customized TCP raw socket listener to mimic low-level banner signatures), we would accept incoming connections asynchronously:
* **Passive Profiling (M-1 Ext.):** Parse user-agent headers, JA3/HTTP-fingerprints (if using TLS/HTTPS), and TLS/SSL cipher suites to identify scanner signatures (e.g., Censys, Shodan, or Go-http-client).
* **Behavioral & Tactical Metrics (M-2 Ext.):** 
  * Track path scanning sequences (e.g., scanning for `/wp-admin`, `.env`, `/api/v1/auth`).
  * Compute request latency patterns (automation detection).
* **Payload Egress & Injection (M-3 Ext.):** 
  * Capture payload bodies containing exploits (e.g., Log4j, Spring4Shell, SQLi, or command injection payloads like `?exec=wget...`).
  * Automatically queue background download of payload files matched in HTTP POST/GET variables (similar to the SSH URL extractor).

#### **C. LLM Interaction & Persona Breakout (M-4 Ext.)**
* If an attacker targets an endpoint interactively (e.g., submitting a login form with arbitrary credentials, interacting with a mock REST API, or executing command-injection probes), the HTTP service will ask the LLM to generate a responsive mock output.
* *Example Prompting Pattern:*
  > "You are a vulnerable router admin interface running on HTTP. An attacker sent a GET request to `/api/system/reboot` with headers [...]. Generate a realistic, convincing JSON response and headers that keeps the attacker engaged."

#### **D. Unified Data Harvesting & Storage**
* Adapt `AuthAttemptLogEntry` and `CommandLogEntry` in the logging subsystem (or create polymorphic equivalents like `HttpRequestLogEntry` and `HttpPayloadLogEntry`) so they output to `frontend/data/harvest.jsonl` in the same structure.
* This ensures that the web-based attacks instantly feed into the same telemetry dashboards and MITRE ATT&CK framework scoring maps.

---

### 3. Estimated Development Effort

We estimate this to be a **Medium effort task (approximately 12–18 hours of engineering time)**, broken down as follows:

| Phase | Description | Estimated Effort |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Phase 1: Config & Boilerplate** | Define `.env` schema, update `AppConfiguration.cs`, and boot a non-blocking `HttpListener` thread inside `Program.cs`. | **2–3 hours** |
| **Phase 2: Ingestion & Telemetry** | Extract HTTP request properties, headers, bodies, IP lookup categories, and pipe them directly into the hot storage tier (`harvest.jsonl`). | **4–5 hours** |
| **Phase 3: LLM Integration** | Craft HTTP-centric system prompts for the LLM simulator, allowing it to mimic various vulnerable systems dynamically based on the requested URI. | **3–4 hours** |
| **Phase 4: Frontend & Reporting** | Add HTTP metrics (e.g., Top Requested Paths, HTTP Status Distribution, User-Agent Profiling) to the static site template. | **3–4 hours** |
| **Phase 5: Testing & Verification** | Write unit and integration tests (using `HttpClient`) to verify that the HTTP honeypot runs safely alongside SSH, records payloads correctly, and invokes the LLM under proper rate limits. | **2–3 hours** |

---

### 4. Trade-offs & Choices to Consider

* **Framework Choice:** Using standard `.NET HttpListener` vs. embedding **Kestrel** (or a raw TCP socket listener). 
  * *HttpListener* is lightweight and requires no external heavy dependencies, keeping the honeypot fast.
  * *Raw TCP socket* lets us emulate low-level TCP signatures (like custom SSL banners) more realistically but increases development complexity.
* **LLM Cost Mitigation:** HTTP scanners send thousands of automated requests per minute. We must strictly apply the `LlmRateLimiter` or use cheaper fallback static responses (e.g., 404/403) for generic paths, reserving LLM generation only for high-signal, interactive requests (e.g., successful exploits, specific API parameters, or forms).

Would you like to focus on drafting the architectural changes for the LLM interaction prompt, or should we look at how to hook the HTTP events into the existing logging pipeline?

Given the **read-only** constraints and the findings from the codebase review, here is a structured implementation plan to modernize FunnyPot. We will focus on improving modularity, concurrency safety, and overall reliability.

### **Phase 1: Foundation & Dependency Management**
*Goal: Remove static global state and improve testability.*
*   **Step 1:** Introduce `Microsoft.Extensions.Hosting` and `Microsoft.Extensions.DependencyInjection`.
*   **Step 2:** Refactor `Program.cs` into distinct, injectable services:
    *   `ConfigurationService`: Encapsulate `AppConfiguration` loading/reading.
    *   `SSHServerService`: Handle server lifecycle (`Start`/`Stop`).
    *   `HoneypotOrchestrator`: Manage session lifecycle and concurrency.
    *   `DataHarvester`: Centralize JSONL logging logic.
*   **Step 3:** Convert static fields into injected dependencies.

### **Phase 2: Concurrency & Stability Upgrades**
*Goal: Eliminate thread-blocking risks and race conditions.*
*   **Step 4:** Replace `httpClient.Send` with `await httpClient.SendAsync` throughout the application to ensure non-blocking network I/O.
*   **Step 5:** Enhance `RotateBanner()`: Implement `ReaderWriterLockSlim` or a similar synchronization primitive to ensure banner transitions are atomic and connection-safe.
*   **Step 6:** Implement a per-session token-bucket rate limiter for the LLM API to prevent cost exhaustion from malicious or high-frequency automated probes.

### **Phase 3: Cleanup & Refactoring**
*Goal: Improve maintainability and adhere to modern patterns.*
*   **Step 7:** Break down the massive `Program.cs` into modular files (`SSHSession.cs`, `LLMClient.cs`, etc.).
*   **Step 8:** Standardize error handling and logging across services.
*   **Step 9:** Add initial unit tests for the `CommandResolver` and `InputValidator` using the new DI structure.

---

### **Implementation Strategy: "Incremental Decoupling"**

We would execute this in small, verifiable steps to ensure the system remains functional at every stage:

1.  **Safety First:** Before any changes, create a suite of unit tests for core logic (`InputValidator`, `ParseBannerPool`, `NormalizeTerminalOutput`) to ensure behavioral parity.
2.  **Incremental DI:** Start by introducing the DI container while keeping the existing static accessors (using them as temporary wrappers) to avoid a "big bang" rewrite.
3.  **Refactor Services:** Extract one service at a time (e.g., first the `Logger`, then `Configuration`) into its own module.
4.  **Async Conversion:** Perform the `HttpClient` switch-over last, once the system is decoupled and easier to test for regressions.

### **Key Trade-off Discussion**
*   **Complexity:** Introducing DI and full modularity will increase the initial boilerplate, but it will significantly lower the cost of future features (like the HTTP honeypot we discussed earlier).
*   **Performance:** Moving to `async/await` throughout will improve throughput, but requires careful attention to potential deadlocks if not handled consistently.

### User / 23/05/2026 09:56:29.200

i dont like async/await pattern suggest another methos (like threadpool)

### Assistant / 23/05/2026 09:56:29.212

I understand the constraint. If you prefer to avoid the `async/await` pattern in C#, we can definitely shift to a **Thread Pool-based orchestration model**. This is a classic pattern that offers high control over concurrency without the overhead or complexity sometimes associated with asynchronous state machines.

Here is how we would adapt the plan for a thread-based approach:

### **The Thread Pool Strategy**

Instead of using `async/await`, we will utilize `Task.Run` and `Thread` primitives to manage background work.

1.  **Work Queueing:** For the LLM response engine, we will use a **Producer-Consumer pattern** with `BlockingCollection<T>`. This allows the SSH session threads to drop "work requests" into a queue without waiting.
2.  **Dedicated Worker Threads:** We will spin up a long-running, dedicated background thread (or a pool) that monitors the `BlockingCollection`, pulls tasks, performs the synchronous network call (`httpClient.Send`), and then signals the session thread when the result is ready.
3.  **Synchronization:** We would replace `await` with `ManualResetEventSlim` or `SemaphoreSlim` for signaling completion back to the original request thread.

### **Revised Architectural Plan (Threaded)**

*   **Phase 1 (Modularity):** We still proceed with the DI refactoring (as it is independent of the concurrency model).
*   **Phase 2 (Threaded Orchestration):**
    *   **HTTP/LLM Integration:** Instead of `await`, each incoming command trigger will create a `Task` (or `Thread`) that is immediately dispatched via `ThreadPool.QueueUserWorkItem`.
    *   **Blocking Operations:** The network calls for the LLM will block their own worker thread. Since the thread pool is elastic, this allows us to handle many concurrent requests without stopping the main SSH server loop.
    *   **Result Delivery:** The worker thread will use a callback mechanism or update a thread-safe dictionary that the SSH session thread polls or waits on via a signaling primitive.

### **Pros and Cons of this Approach**

| Aspect | `async/await` (Existing) | Thread Pool (Proposed) |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Simplicity** | High-level, but complex state machines. | Lower-level, predictable flow control. |
| **Resources** | Low memory footprint (no stacks). | Higher (each thread has a stack). |
| **Complexity** | Harder to debug (stack traces). | Easier to debug (linear stack). |
| **Deadlocks** | Possible if `SyncContext` is misused. | Possible if locks are mismanaged. |

This method gives you much more explicit control over how resources are allocated to the honeypot tasks, and it avoids the "viral" nature of `async/await`.

Does this thread-centric model align better with your architecture design, or would you like to explore another concurrency model like **Event-Driven/Reactor pattern** (using `Select` or `Poll` on sockets)?

*   *Fixed-size pool:* Safer for API limits and resource control (prevents runaway threads).
*   *Per-session threads:* Easier to code, but potentially resource-heavy if hundreds of connections are accepted simultaneously.

How would you like to handle this resource constraint?

Since we are in **Plan Mode**, I have updated our roadmap to reflect this architecture.

### **Finalized Plan: Threaded & Modular Refactoring**

1.  **Refactoring to Dependency Injection (DI):**
    *   Transition `Program.cs` to a `Host`-based architecture using `Microsoft.Extensions.Hosting`.
    *   Extract core functionality into modular, injectable services (e.g., `ConfigurationService`, `SSHServerService`, `Orchestrator`).
2.  **Per-Session Threaded Orchestration:**
    *   Replace `async/await` patterns with `ThreadPool.QueueUserWorkItem` or dedicated `new Thread()` dispatching for LLM requests.
    *   Utilize thread-safe signaling (e.g., `ManualResetEventSlim`) to synchronize the worker thread's result back to the specific session thread.
3.  **Synchronization Safety:**
    *   Use `ReaderWriterLockSlim` for the `RotateBanner` operation to ensure atomicity.
    *   Continue using `ConcurrentDictionary` for session state, as this is compatible with both `async` and threaded models.
4.  **Resource Controls:**
    *   Implement per-session rate limiting (Token Bucket) to ensure that even with per-session threading, a single session cannot consume excessive API credits or cause resource exhaustion.

### Assistant / 23/05/2026 09:42:03.844

This high-level review of the **FunnyPot** codebase identifies key architectural patterns, stability risks, security considerations, and concurrency implementations.

### 1. Architectural Patterns
FunnyPot utilizes an asynchronous, event-driven architecture heavily reliant on `FxSsh` for SSH handling and `System.Threading.Tasks` for LLM interaction and data harvesting.
*   **Interaction Flow:** Connections are accepted by an SSH server (`FxSsh`), which registers services (auth/connection). Upon authentication, a shell session is initialized that orchestrates input validation, command resolution, and LLM-based response generation.
*   **Strengths:** The separation of Concerns between the `SSHServer`, `CommandResolver`, and `DataHarvester` provides a clean structure. The use of `FakeFileSystem` to maintain state across a session's lifetime is an effective pattern for a honeypot.
*   **Areas for Improvement:** The reliance on `Program.cs` as a "God Class" containing high-level configuration, state management, and orchestration is a maintainability bottleneck. Moving orchestration logic into dedicated `SessionManager` or `HoneypotOrchestrator` classes would improve modularity.

### 2. Stability and Error Handling
*   **Bottlenecks:** The `HttpClient` usage in `GetLLMResponse` is synchronous (`httpClient.Send(requestMessage)`), which can block thread pool threads if the API is slow, potentially starving the SSH server's ability to process new connections under heavy load.
*   **Rate Limiting:** The code relies on `SemaphoreSlim(MaxSessions)` to bound concurrency, which is robust. However, there is no explicit rate limiting for LLM requests per session, leaving the system susceptible to cost exhaustion or API rate limiting if an attacker rapidly sends commands.
*   **Error Handling:** The current `try-catch` blocks in `GetLLMResponse` and `OnConnectionAccepted` are good, but they log and continue without alerting or gracefully failing the session if API services are down.

### 3. Security
*   **Secrets:** Credentials and API keys are retrieved using `GetSecretOrEnvironment`, which is a secure pattern, checking `/run/secrets` first.
*   **Robustness:** The use of `InputValidator` to block null bytes and repetitive noise is a good defense-in-depth measure against injection and resource exhaustion attacks.
*   **SSH Host Keys:** The logic in `GetHostKeyPem` successfully implements persistent RSA key generation, which is crucial for preventing "Man-in-the-Middle" alerts on subsequent connections.
*   **Risk:** Running LLM requests directly within the `Channel.DataReceived` path means that any latency in the LLM response is experienced directly by the client. An attacker could potentially use this to slow down the system (Denial of Service).

### 4. Concurrency
*   **Usage of `ConcurrentDictionary`:** Extensive use of `ConcurrentDictionary` for session state (e.g., `AuthAttempts`, `ShellAnalyticsBySession`) is appropriate for this high-concurrency environment.
*   **SemaphoreSlim:** Correctly used to bound total concurrent sessions.
*   **Race Conditions:** The `RotateBanner` function has a potential race condition. While `Volatile.Read` checks `_activeConnections`, there is no guarantee that between the check and the call to `StartSshServer`, new connections won't arrive. A more robust approach might be to queue connection requests or temporarily stop accepting new connections during rotation.

### 5. Code Quality and Modern Patterns
*   **Strengths:** Good usage of C# features like `async/await`, `Interlocked`, and `Guid` to manage concurrent state. The YAML logging provides highly valuable structured data for analysis.
*   **Maintainability:** The project lacks dependency injection (DI). Transitioning to a standard `Microsoft.Extensions.Hosting` approach with DI would drastically improve testability and modularity, as services like `HttpClient`, `AppConfiguration`, and `Logger` are currently static.
*   **Modernization:** The use of `.NET 8.0` is current and beneficial. The codebase is generally idiomatic, but the large `Program.cs` file (1200+ lines) is a significant technical debt that should be addressed to ease future development.

### Recommendations
1.  **Refactor `Program.cs`:** Split this file into a proper multi-class structure (e.g., `Orchestrator`, `SessionHandler`, `ConfigManager`).
2.  **Asynchronous API Calls:** Switch `httpClient.Send` to `await httpClient.SendAsync` to prevent thread blocking.
3.  **Implement DI:** Introduce `Microsoft.Extensions.DependencyInjection` to move away from static global state.
4.  **Add LLM Rate Limiting:** Introduce a per-session rate limit (e.g., token-bucket algorithm) to guard against API abuse.
5.  **Strengthen Banner Rotation:** Use a `ReaderWriterLockSlim` or an `AsyncSemaphore` to ensure safe, atomic transitions during banner rotation.

### Assistant / 24/05/2026 12:49:23.426

That entry was not really an LLM response.

The command starts with `cd`, so the resolver treated the whole payload as a simple builtin `cd` command:

Because `cd` succeeds silently in Bash, the honeypot returned empty output, marked `ok`, and logged `0ms`. That explains the confusing UI: the rest of the malware download/execution chain was never sent to the LLM.

I fixed it:
- Compound shell commands with `;`, `&&`, `||`, or `|` now bypass builtin/static shortcuts.
- Those commands will go to the LLM branch instead.

### Assistant / 24/05/2026 15:59:06.389

No, that response is not correct.

For:

`echo 1 > /dev/null && cat /bin/echo`

A real shell would:
- Write `1` to `/dev/null`, so nothing visible appears.
- Because `echo` succeeds, run `cat /bin/echo`.
- `cat /bin/echo` would dump binary executable bytes, typically beginning with ELF-like binary junk, not just `/`.

I tightened the LLM prompt for this class of probe:
- Explicitly honor redirection and `&&`.
- Do not echo redirected output.
- For `cat` on binaries like `/bin/echo`, output plausible `\x7fELF...` binary junk/truncated output.
- Never replace binary file contents with only a path or single `/`.

Added a unit test to lock those prompt instructions in.

## Goal
- Debug and improve FunnyPot SSH honeypot command response handling, especially LLM routing/realism, then prepare product results materials and investigate missing UI command responses.

## Constraints & Preferences
- Prefer LLM to answer most attacker commands; avoid over-expanding deterministic branches.
- It is acceptable and desirable for LLM to hallucinate plausible miner/suspicious-process output to learn attacker TTPs.

## Progress
### Done
- Fixed raw LLM API/network failures being returned to attackers:
  - Added `CommandResolver.IsModelFailureResponse(...)`.
  - Added `CommandResolver.GenerateLocalFallbackResponse(...)`.
  - Removed failed LLM user message pollution.

  - `.env` key present in repo.

  - Uses configured OpenRouter URL/model/max tokens via `Config`.
  - Adds `HTTP-Referer` and `X-Title`.

  - Case-insensitive JSON properties for lowercase `command`/`response`.

### Assistant / 24/05/2026 19:53:05.511

The live container confirms the UI’s “no result recorded yet” is real for recent `cat /bin/echo` attempts from `8.131.74.250`, not just stale snapshot data. That points to the backend logging the command event, then failing or closing before `command_result` is emitted for binary-output commands.

### Assistant / 24/05/2026 20:02:06.066

Root cause found: `Program.cs` sends the honeypot response to the SSH channel before writing the `command_result` event. For these `cat /bin/echo` sessions, the LLM timed out at 30s, fallback generated a response, then the attacker disconnected during/around send, so the response was logged in app logs but never made it into harvest JSONL. I’m going to move result harvesting before channel writes and add a deterministic local response for `cat /bin/echo` so binary probes don’t wait on the LLM.

### Assistant / 24/05/2026 20:03:06.476

The patch now records `command_result` before any SSH channel write can fail, and `cat /bin/echo` is handled locally with ELF-like truncated binary output instead of waiting on OpenRouter. I’m running the test suite now.

### Assistant / 24/05/2026 20:03:26.546

### Assistant / 24/05/2026 20:03:36.335

The live container still has the old code until rebuilt. I’m going to rebuild and restart the service so new command results are captured immediately.

### Assistant / 24/05/2026 20:05:12.601

Fixed and deployed the missing command-result issue.

- `FunnyPot/Program.cs`: logs `command_result` before writing to the SSH channel, so attacker disconnects can’t prevent harvest records from being saved.

### User / 25/05/2026 09:17:30.553

### Assistant / 25/05/2026 09:17:30.603

I’ll ground the plan in the current project map first, then propose an external review workflow that can inspect telemetry, understand FunnyPot behavior, and produce safe code-change recommendations or patches.

### Assistant / 25/05/2026 09:17:34.892

**Recommended Shape**
Build this as an external “review agent” project, not inside FunnyPot runtime code.

Location options:
1. Separate repo: `FunnyPot-Reviewer`
2. Separate directory outside repo: `/home/felix/FunnyPot-Reviewer`
3. Separate branch/tooling directory in current repo: less ideal because you asked separate from codebase

Recommendation: separate repo or sibling directory, with read-only access to FunnyPot plus optional controlled patch output.

**Goal**
Periodically review attacker commands and responses, decide whether FunnyPot behavior should improve, and produce one of:

- No change needed
- Static response dataset update
- Prompt/routing improvement
- Bug report with evidence
- Patch/PR candidate
- High-risk finding that requires manual review

**Core Loop**
1. Collect telemetry:
- `frontend/data/harvest.jsonl`
- `frontend/data/harvest_summary.json`
- `frontend/sessions/*.log`
- container logs if needed
- git history and current source snapshot

2. Identify review candidates:
- commands with no `command_result`
- empty responses
- suspiciously short responses like `/`
- `[api error]` or `[network error]`
- long response latency/timeouts
- failed commands that should probably succeed
- repeated attacker commands not handled well
- high-value TTP commands: payload download, persistence, crypto miners, recon, credential theft

3. Classify root cause:
- UI pairing issue
- backend didn’t log result
- LLM timeout/failure
- deterministic shortcut too aggressive
- static response missing or bad
- prompt quality issue
- expected shell behavior already acceptable

4. Decide improvement type:
- Add/adjust deterministic built-in only for simple shell semantics
- Add fallback behavior for common failure modes
- Improve prompt if behavior is semantic/LLM-related
- Add test coverage for regression
- Leave as-is if LLM hallucination is useful or realistic enough

5. Produce output:
- Markdown review report
- JSON findings file
- optional patch file
- optional branch/commit/PR only if configured

**Important Guardrails**
The reviewer should not directly mutate production by default.

Default mode:
- Read telemetry
- Read source
- Generate report
- Generate patch proposal in a separate output folder

Apply mode:
- Create a branch
- Apply patch
- Run `dotnet test`
- Run `graphify update .`
- Optionally commit
- Never push/restart Docker unless explicitly enabled

**Context Package**
To “have all the context” while remaining separate, give the reviewer a context bundle:

- Current git commit hash
- `graphify-out/GRAPH_REPORT.md`
- selected `graphify-out/graph.json` metadata
- `FunnyPot/Program.cs`
- `FunnyPot.Tests/UnitTests.cs`
- `FunnyPot/data/ssh_responses.jsonl`
- `config/app-config.yaml`
- recent telemetry snapshot
- previous review reports
- project policy file describing desired behavior

The policy file is important. Example principles:
- Prefer LLM for complex attacker commands.
- Do not hardcode every observed command.
- Deterministic handling is okay for shell basics, binary `cat`, redirects, `cd`, `echo`, `pwd`, etc.
- Empty/no-result command responses are bugs unless clearly expected.
- LLM hallucination of plausible malware/miner artifacts is acceptable when it elicits attacker TTPs.
- Every code change needs tests.

**Suggested Directory**
Sibling tool:

```text
/home/felix/FunnyPot-Reviewer/
  config.yaml
  README.md
  policies/
    behavior-policy.md
  scripts/
    collect_context.sh
    analyze_telemetry.py
    run_review.py
    apply_patch.sh
  prompts/
    reviewer-system.md
    finding-template.md
  output/
    reviews/
    patches/
    snapshots/
```

**Review Report Format**
Each run should produce something like:

```markdown
# FunnyPot Attacker Command Review

Commit reviewed: <hash>
Telemetry window: <start> - <end>

## Findings

### High
- Missing command_result for `cat /bin/echo`
- Evidence: 3 commands, no results, app log fallback generated response
- Cause: command_result logged after channel send
- Recommended fix: log result before send
- Test: add regression for binary cat handling

### Medium
- Empty response for compound downloader command
- Evidence: repeated empty results
- Cause: old static/builtin short-circuit
- Status: already fixed in commit X

## No Change
- Miner process hallucination accepted because it improves attacker engagement.

## Proposed Changes
- files changed
- tests to run
```

**Automation Schedule**
Good first version:

- Run every 6 hours via cron or systemd timer.
- Review last 24 hours of telemetry.
- Keep rolling `output/reviews/YYYY-MM-DD-HHMM.md`.
- Notify via ntfy/email/GitHub issue only when findings are medium/high.

Later version:
- GitHub Action or external runner.
- Opens PR automatically for low-risk changes.
- Creates GitHub issue for high-risk changes.
- Dashboard card showing “reviewer found N improvement candidates”.

**Implementation Phases**
1. Manual report generator:
- Collect telemetry and summarize anomalies.
- No LLM patching yet.
- Output Markdown/JSON report.

2. LLM-assisted analysis:
- Feed anomaly summary plus context bundle.
- Ask for classified findings and recommended fix type.

3. Patch proposal:
- Generate patch files only.
- Human applies/reviews.

4. Safe auto-fix:
- Create branch.
- Apply patch.
- Run tests.
- Run graphify.
- Commit locally.

5. Optional deployment:
- Push branch or PR.
- Restart Docker only after explicit approval or trusted mode.

**Key Design Decision**
I would not let the periodic reviewer directly change `main` or restart Docker automatically at first.

Best workflow:
- Reviewer creates a report and optional branch.
- You approve.
- Then we commit/push/restart.

After it proves reliable, we can allow auto-PRs for low-risk categories like:
- test additions
- static response typo fixes
- graph freshness updates
- non-runtime docs/report updates

**One Caveat**
Graph freshness currently may again be stale because the latest freshness commit changed `HEAD`. For the reviewer, we should compare graph freshness against the target code commit carefully, or record both:
- `code_commit_reviewed`
- `graph_built_from_commit`

That avoids false alarms when graph-only commits move `HEAD`.

### User / 25/05/2026 18:13:56.819

Does the llm checks the edicquency of the responses to the attackers command?

### Assistant / 25/05/2026 18:13:56.853

Not strongly enough yet.

Current reviewer flow:
- Deterministic analyzer checks structural/runtime issues:
  - missing `command_result`
  - empty unexpected responses
  - suspiciously short responses
  - raw API/network errors
  - slow responses
  - repeated command shapes
- LLM stage receives:
  - deterministic findings
  - recent command/result events
  - graph/config/source excerpts
  - policy

But because the current post-deploy window has `0` attacker commands, the LLM did not evaluate adequacy of actual command responses.

Also, the prompt asks generally whether FunnyPot needs code/prompt/config changes, but it does not explicitly require a per-command “response adequacy” review.

We should improve it by adding a specific LLM rubric:

- Does the response look like realistic Linux shell output?
- Does it answer the exact command semantics?
- Is it too generic, too short, or misleading?
- Did deterministic routing bypass LLM when it should not?
- Would this response keep an attacker engaged?
- Should this be fixed by prompt, static response, fallback, routing, or no change?
- For each suspicious pair, classify: `adequate`, `poor realism`, `wrong semantics`, `too slow`, `missing`, `expected no output`.

And feed it recent command/result pairs even from the wider telemetry window, while marking whether they are pre-deploy or post-deploy.
