## Code Review: FunnyPot SSH Honeypot

### CRITICAL BUGS

**1. YAML config file is completely ignored -- naming convention mismatch**
`FunnyPot/AppConfiguration.cs:38` uses `HyphenatedNamingConvention`, which expects keys like `ssh:`, `max-sessions:`, `auth-max-tries:`. But `config/app-config.yaml` uses PascalCase (`SSH:`, `MaxSessions:`, `AuthMaxTries:`). YamlDotNet is case-sensitive with naming conventions, so **every key fails to bind** and all values silently fall back to C# defaults.

The test at `UnitTests.cs:546` (`Load_ProjectConfig_BindsRootSections`) masks this by asserting values identical to the defaults -- it passes whether the config loads or not.

```yaml
# What app-config.yaml has:       # What it should have:
SSH:                                ssh:
  MaxSessions: 50                     max-sessions: 50
  AuthMaxTries: 3                     auth-max-tries: 3
LLM:                                llm:
  DelayMs: 500                        delay-ms: 500
```

**2. `int.Parse` crashes on invalid environment variables**
Three locations use `int.Parse` instead of `TryParse`, causing `FormatException` on startup if the env var is set to a non-integer:

- `Program.cs:205` -- `int.Parse(Environment.GetEnvironmentVariable("SSH_BANNER_ROTATION_INTERVAL") ?? "0")`
- `Program.cs:1449` -- `int.Parse(Environment.GetEnvironmentVariable("LLM_RATE_LIMIT_MAX") ?? "20")`
- `Program.cs:1450` -- `int.Parse(Environment.GetEnvironmentVariable("LLM_RATE_LIMIT_WINDOW_SECONDS") ?? "60")`
- `Program.cs:2415` -- `int.Parse(Environment.GetEnvironmentVariable("DATA_PUSH_INTERVAL_SECONDS") ?? "300")`

The codebase already has `GetIntEnvironmentOrDefault` that handles this correctly -- these should use it.

**3. Banner rotation index overflow causes crash**
`Program.cs:135`:
```csharp
var nextIndex = Interlocked.Increment(ref _currentBannerIndex) % BannerPool.Count;
```
After ~2.1 billion rotations, `_currentBannerIndex` wraps to `int.MinValue`. The `%` operator on a negative number produces a negative result, causing `IndexOutOfRangeException` on `BannerPool[nextIndex]`.

**4. `ResolvePath("..")` returns empty string instead of `"/"`**
`Program.cs:1290`:
```csharp
if (path == "..")
    return CurrentDirectory == "/" ? "/" : CurrentDirectory[..CurrentDirectory.LastIndexOf('/')];
```
When `CurrentDirectory` is `"/home"`, `LastIndexOf('/')` returns `0`, so `CurrentDirectory[..0]` returns `""`. This breaks path resolution for `cd ..` from a top-level directory. Fix: return `"/"` when the slice would be empty.

---

### SECURITY / OPSEC ISSUES (honeypot leaking real host info)

**5. `who` and `last` commands leak real OS username**
`Program.cs:1814` and `Program.cs:2013`:
```csharp
// who command
response = $"{Environment.UserName}   pts/0 ...";
// last command
return $"{Environment.UserName}   pts/0 ...";
```
`Environment.UserName` returns the actual host OS username (e.g., `test` from the Docker container or your real user on bare metal). An attacker running `who` or `last` can see this is not a real server. Should be hardcoded to `"remote"`.

**6. `date` command lies about timezone**
`Program.cs:1810`:
```csharp
response = DateTime.Now.ToString("ddd MMM dd HH:mm:ss UTC yyyy");
```
Uses `DateTime.Now` (local time) but hardcodes "UTC" in the format string. The output claims UTC but the hours/minutes are local. Should use `DateTime.UtcNow`.

**7. `SCPDetector` also blocks legitimate `ssh` commands**
`Program.cs:1160`:
```csharp
private static readonly Regex SftpPattern = new(@"(?i)^\s*(sftp|ssh|sftp\-server)", ...);
```
This pattern matches `ssh` in addition to `sftp`. Any command starting with `ssh` (e.g., `ssh user@host`, `ssh -D 1080 host`) is blocked as an "SCP command." This reduces honeypot fidelity and prevents the LLM from responding to SSH tunneling attempts that the `DataHarvester` is specifically designed to detect.

---

### CONCURRENCY BUGS

**8. `PushToGit` holds the global logging lock during network I/O**
`Program.cs:2691`: `PushToGit` acquires `lock (_lock)` -- the same lock used by `LogMsg` (line 2657) and `LogYaml` (line 2438). A git push can take many seconds. During that time, **all logging and YAML event writing is blocked**, stalling active SSH sessions that need to log commands/responses.

**9. `HarvestedCredentials` list is not thread-safe**
`Program.cs:393-404`:
```csharp
var harvested = HarvestedCredentials.GetOrAdd(sessionKey, _ => new List<HarvestedCredential>());
harvested.Add(new HarvestedCredential { ... });
```
`ConcurrentDictionary.GetOrAdd` is thread-safe, but the returned `List<T>` is not. Concurrent auth attempts for the same session key can corrupt the list.

**10. `SessionCommandWorker.Dispose` can dispose queue while worker thread is still running**
`Program.cs:1441-1442`:
```csharp
_thread.Join(TimeSpan.FromSeconds(2));
_queue.Dispose();
```
If the thread doesn't join within 2 seconds (e.g., a slow LLM call), the background thread will throw `ObjectDisposedException` when it tries to dequeue the next item.

**11. `StaticResponseStore._loaded` lacks volatile**
`Program.cs:1900-1905`: Classic double-checked locking with a plain `bool` field. Without `volatile`, the first read outside the lock can see a stale `false` on architectures with weak memory ordering (ARM). On x86 this works by accident.

---

### LOGIC BUGS

**12. `IsFailureResponse` is far too broad**
`Program.cs:2282`:
```csharp
return lower.Contains("command not found") || lower.Contains("permission denied") 
    || lower.Contains("no such file") || lower.Contains("error") || lower.Contains("failed");
```
The `"error"` and `"failed"` substring checks match any output containing those words -- `cat /var/log/error.log`, `grep "error handling"`, process listings mentioning errors, etc. This heavily inflates the `FailedCommands` counter and corrupts `StandardErrorRatio` analytics.

**13. `CategorizeInfrastructure` breaks on IPv6 endpoints**
`Program.cs:2192`:
```csharp
var host = remoteEndpoint.Split(':')[0];
```
For IPv6 like `[2001:db8::1]:49152`, this splits on the first `:` yielding `[2001`, which fails `IPAddress.TryParse`. The codebase has `GetRemoteAttemptKey` which handles this correctly -- should reuse it.

**14. Built-in `type`/`which`/`getconf` return null on missing args**
`Program.cs:1724-1754`: When these commands are invoked without arguments (e.g., just `type`), `response` stays `null` but `return true` is hit. The caller uses `builtinResponse!` which is null, so the channel sends `"\r\n"` instead of a proper usage message.

**15. `echo` built-in doesn't strip shell quotes**
`Program.cs:1704`:
```csharp
response = string.Join(" ", parts.Skip(1));
```
`echo "hello world"` outputs `"hello world"` (with literal quotes). A real shell strips them. Reduces fidelity.

**16. `IsCompoundShellCommand` doesn't respect quoting**
`Program.cs:1616`:
```csharp
return Regex.IsMatch(command, @"&&|\|\||;|(?<!\|)\|(?!\|)");
```
`echo "hello; world"` is classified as a compound command and sent to the LLM instead of being handled by the fast built-in `echo`.

**17. Exec command bypasses `SessionCommandWorker` serialization**
`Program.cs:765`:
```csharp
ProcessLine(args.CommandText ?? "");
```
For exec (non-interactive) commands, `ProcessLine` is called directly on the event handler thread, not via `commandWorker.TryPost()`. The worker is created at line 713 but unused for the initial command. This bypasses the thread-safety guarantee the worker provides.

---

### RESOURCE LEAKS / MEMORY

**18. `LlmRateLimiter` entries never expire**
`Program.cs:1448`: `IpLimits` is a `ConcurrentDictionary` that grows with every unique session/IP. Entries are only removed via explicit `Reset()` calls (which only happen in tests). In production, this grows unboundedly.

**19. Static field initialization accesses Config before `.env` files are loaded**
`Program.cs:27-34`: All the `static readonly` fields (`AuthMaxTries`, `MaxSessions`, `SshPort`, etc.) are initialized during class load, which triggers `Config` loading. But `Main()` calls `LoadDotEnvFiles()` at line 171 **after** static initialization. So `.env` file values for `AUTH_MAX_TRIES`, `SSH_PORT`, etc. are ignored -- only actual process environment variables (set before launch) take effect.

---

### MINOR / STYLE

**20. `EnrichHarvestWithMetricResponses` redundant condition**
`Program.cs:2794-2798`:
```csharp
if (!changed)
    return false;
if (changed)        // <-- always true at this point
    File.WriteAllLines(harvestPath, lines);
```

**21. `.env` file comments in config**
`config/app-config.yaml:57-58`: Git config comments say "Set via GITHUB_TOKEN env var" for the User field and "Set via GITHUB_USER env var" for the Token field -- these are swapped.

---

| Severity | Count |
|----------|-------|
| Critical (crash/wrong behavior) | 4 |
| Security/OPSEC (breaks honeypot cover) | 3 |
| Concurrency | 4 |
| Logic bugs | 6 |
| Resource leaks | 2 |
| Minor | 2 |

The most impactful issue is **#1** -- the YAML config file is entirely ignored due to the naming convention mismatch, meaning the app runs purely on hardcoded defaults. This also means any config tuning done in `app-config.yaml` has zero effect.

### User / 26/05/2026 08:49:13.932

Are the binary payloads sent to the honeypot saved in any way?

### Assistant / 26/05/2026 08:49:13.951

I’ll inspect the project graph first, then trace the honeypot payload handling paths to see whether raw binary data is persisted.

### Assistant / 26/05/2026 08:49:27.968

The implementation is concentrated in `FunnyPot/Program.cs`; I’m narrowing on command logging, SCP detection, and payload harvesting rather than assuming “payload” means only HTTP URLs.

### Assistant / 26/05/2026 08:49:50.614

Yes, but only in limited forms.

- Shell input is saved as text, not raw bytes: `channel.DataReceived` decodes incoming bytes with `Encoding.UTF8.GetString(data)`, buffers until newline, then logs the resulting command string to app logs, YAML, metrics, and harvest data. See `FunnyPot/Program.cs:735` and `FunnyPot/Program.cs:598-626`.

- Binary/null-byte input is blocked, but after command logging: `InputValidator.Validate` rejects null bytes with `"Binary content not allowed"` at `FunnyPot/Program.cs:1134-1142`, but validation happens after `Logger.LogMsg` and `Logger.LogYaml("command", ...)`.

- Payload URLs are not saved as binary files: commands containing `http://` or `https://` trigger `CapturePayloadMetadataAsync`, which downloads up to 10 MB only to compute `BytesCaptured` and `Sha256`; it does not write the response body to disk. See `FunnyPot/Program.cs:628-629` and `FunnyPot/Program.cs:2328-2389`.

- SCP/SFTP is blocked in the live command path: `SCPDetector.IsSCPCommand` causes `"Operation not allowed"` before command resolution. See `FunnyPot/Program.cs:641-650`.

- There is an `SCPUploadHandler.CaptureUpload` that would write uploaded bytes to `LogDir/uploads/...` using `File.WriteAllBytes`, but I don’t see it called anywhere. See `FunnyPot/Program.cs:1210-1262`.

So: raw binary payload bodies fetched from URLs are not persisted; actual SSH input is persisted only after UTF-8 decoding as logged text; SCP upload byte-saving code exists but appears unused.

### User / 26/05/2026 17:36:00.348

The uptime is always 47d 3hrs 22 minutes... Suggest ways to show real physical uptime without interfacing to the real system

### Assistant / 26/05/2026 17:36:00.377

I’ll ground this in the current code map first, then suggest options that preserve honeypot isolation while avoiding a static uptime fingerprint.

### Assistant / 26/05/2026 17:36:11.249

Yes. Don’t expose the real host/container uptime directly; expose a controlled “believable uptime” derived from an internal synthetic boot time.

Best options:

1. Synthetic boot time persisted to disk
- On first app start, generate `fake_boot_time = now - random(14..180 days)`.
- Save it to a small state file, e.g. `${LOG_DIR}/persona_state.json`.
- Every `uptime`, `uptime -p`, `/proc/uptime`, `who -b`, etc. calculates from that timestamp.
- Survives Docker restarts if `LOG_DIR` is mounted.
- Does not query the real system.
- Best default.

2. Synthetic boot time from deployment start plus offset
- At app startup, set `fake_boot_time = app_start_time - random(30..120 days)`.
- Uptime increases naturally while the process runs.
- Simple, but resets to a new fake value on container restart unless persisted.

3. Session-stable uptime
- Generate a fake boot time per attacker session.
- Every command inside that session is consistent.
- Different attackers see different host ages.
- Useful for deception, but less realistic if the same actor reconnects and compares.

4. Configured persona uptime
- Add config/env vars:
  - `FAKE_UPTIME_MIN_DAYS=30`
  - `FAKE_UPTIME_MAX_DAYS=180`
  - `FAKE_UPTIME_STATE_PATH=/var/log/funnypot/persona_state.json`
- Lets you tune whether the honeypot looks neglected, freshly rebooted, or long-lived.

5. Drifted synthetic uptime
- Persist fake boot time, but add a tiny deterministic jitter to displayed minutes/seconds.
- Example: uptime output changes naturally, but not perfectly tied to wall-clock seconds.
- Avoids a robotic exact timestamp while staying internally consistent.

I’d implement option 1: persisted synthetic boot time. It gives realistic, increasing uptime without touching `/proc/uptime`, `Environment.TickCount`, host APIs, Docker APIs, or the real OS. Also update all related responses together so `uptime`, `uptime -p`, `/proc/uptime`, and prompt/LLM instructions don’t contradict each other.

### User / 26/05/2026 21:00:16.995

Implement option 1.

### Assistant / 26/05/2026 21:00:17.026

I’ll replace the hard-coded uptime persona with a persisted synthetic boot timestamp, then route local uptime responses and LLM prompt guidance through that state.

### Assistant / 26/05/2026 21:00:49.111

I’m adding a small `SyntheticHostClock` state component that stores `persona_state.json` in `LOG_DIR`, then using it for `uptime`, fallback `uptime`, `top`, and prompt text.

### Assistant / 26/05/2026 21:01:56.430

### User / 27/05/2026 06:11:03.469

Why does mittre show command and control 5 items, there's almost none of it, most actions are reconnaissance

### Assistant / 27/05/2026 06:11:03.500

I’ll check the harvested events and current classifier behavior to confirm whether the count is coming from real `curl/wget/tftp/ssh -D` matches, session-level aggregation, or older `T1105` records being normalized to `Command and Control`.

### Assistant / 27/05/2026 06:11:44.266

It’s showing `Command and Control: 5` because of two implementation details:

1. The MITRE bar counts **sessions**, not commands.
   `session.tactics` is a `Set`, so one session with one `wget/curl` command contributes `1` to `Command and Control`, no matter how many recon commands happened.

2. Historical `T1105` values are normalized to `Command and Control`.
   We mapped `T1105` / `T1090` to `Command and Control`, so old download/tool-transfer commands inflate that tactic box.

The bigger issue: most recon-looking shell commands are currently under-classified. Commands like `uname`, `ifconfig`, `uptime`, `/ip cloud print` in older logs often had `MitreAttackTechniques: []`, because the backend only marked `Discovery` for narrow indicators like `/etc/passwd`, `.ssh`, `.env`, `/etc/shadow`, secrets, etc.

So the dashboard isn’t saying “most commands are C2”. It’s saying “5 sessions had at least one command mapped to C2, while many recon commands were not classified.”

Best fix:
- Count tactics per **command**, not per session, in the MITRE bars.
- Expand discovery/recon classification for `uname`, `whoami`, `id`, `hostname`, `ifconfig`, `ip addr`, `netstat`, `ss`, `ps`, `ls`, `pwd`, `/ip ...`, etc.
- Optionally backfill old log records so historical `/ip cloud print` entries show `Reconnaissance` or `Discovery`.

### Assistant / 27/05/2026 13:58:45.418

I’ll check the project map first, then trace how command responses are classified and timed so the explanation matches this codebase rather than guessing.

### Assistant / 27/05/2026 13:59:14.948

The key path is the command resolver: static response first, then LLM, then local fallback only for API/network failures. I’m reading that section plus the failure/timing logging.

### Assistant / 27/05/2026 14:14:51.554

This is expected from the current resolver behavior.

**Why `/ip cloud print` failed twice**

`/ip cloud print` is a MikroTik/RouterOS command, not Linux. FunnyPot detects `/ip ...` as a non-Linux network-device probe and immediately returns local fallback:

`bash: /ip: No such file or directory`

That is classified as failed because `DataHarvester.IsFailureResponse()` treats `no such file or directory` as failure.

Relevant code:

`FunnyPot/Program.cs:1911-1914` detects `/ip ...`.

`FunnyPot/Program.cs:2039` returns `bash: /ip: No such file or directory`.

`FunnyPot/Program.cs:2732-2735` marks that text as failure.

**Why `ifconfig` took 19s / 32s**

`ifconfig` is not currently handled as a built-in/static response. It falls through to the LLM path:

`FunnyPot/Program.cs:1938`

So the duration shown is mostly OpenRouter/LLM latency, not local execution time.

That is why one attacker got:

`ok response 19,312ms`

The LLM returned plausible interface output.

And another got:

`failed response 32,075ms`

The LLM returned:

`bash: ifconfig: command not found`

That text is then classified as failure by the same failure regex.

**Why `cat /proc/cpuinfo` has “no result recorded yet”**

The dashboard shows this when it has a `command` event but cannot find a matching `command_result` event:

`frontend/index.html:1194`

Commands are logged before the response is resolved:

`FunnyPot/Program.cs:607-628`

The result is only logged after `ResolveCommand()` finishes:

`FunnyPot/Program.cs:703-718`

So for `cat /proc/cpuinfo`, the most likely causes are:

1. It was still waiting on the LLM when the dashboard loaded.
2. The session/app stopped or disconnected before `command_result` was written.
3. The LLM call hung or took a very long time.

Also, `cat /proc/cpuinfo` is not static/built-in right now, so it also goes to the LLM path.

**Short version**

`/ip cloud print` is fast local fallback and intentionally failed. `ifconfig` and `cat /proc/cpuinfo` go to the LLM, so they can be slow or inconsistent. “No result recorded yet” means the dashboard saw the input command but no matching result event was logged yet.

### Assistant / 28/05/2026 08:00:35.397

The “name next to the IP” in **Interactive Sessions** is the session’s `infra` label, not a cryptocurrency lookup.

In current code it comes from:

- `frontend/index.html:920`: `model.infra = data.InfrastructureCategory || model.infra`
- `FunnyPot/Program.cs:402`: auth attempts write `InfrastructureCategory`
- `FunnyPot/Program.cs:2643`: `CategorizeInfrastructure()` currently only returns `private`, `hosting_cloud`, or `unknown`

So if you’re seeing actual crypto names like Bitcoin/Monero/etc, they are almost certainly already present in old/stale `harvest.jsonl` telemetry, not generated by the current code. The dashboard replays stored events, so old labels persist even after the classifier changes.

Likely fixes:

1. Clean/regenerate `frontend/data/harvest.jsonl` if the old labels are bad data.
2. Add frontend normalization so only expected infra categories render, and unexpected values show as `unknown`.
3. Rename that second line under Remote to make clear it is “infra category”, not IP geolocation.

### User / 28/05/2026 08:04:08.096

Based on what are those labels generated, what do those names represent

### Assistant / 28/05/2026 08:04:08.104

I’m checking the exact IP classification helper so I can answer from the implemented logic, not guess.

### Assistant / 28/05/2026 08:04:27.095

Those labels are generated from the source IP’s `InfrastructureCategory`.

In the current code, they are based only on the remote endpoint IP:

- `FunnyPot/Program.cs:340` extracts the IP from `RemoteEndpoint`.
- `FunnyPot/Program.cs:2643` classifies it with `DataHarvester.CategorizeInfrastructure()`.
- `frontend/index.html:920` copies `data.InfrastructureCategory` into the session model.
- `frontend/index.html:1233` renders it under the IP in **Interactive Sessions**.

Current meanings:

- `private`: loopback or private LAN IPs like `127.0.0.1`, `10.x.x.x`, `192.168.x.x`, `172.16-31.x.x`
- `hosting_cloud`: rough heuristic for common cloud/hosting IPv4 ranges, based on first octet only: `3`, `13`, `18`, `20`, `34`, `35`, `40`, `44`, `52`, `54`, `104`, `138`, `139`
- `unknown`: not parseable, IPv6, or not matched by the above heuristic

The current code does **not** generate cryptocurrency names. If you are seeing names like `Bitcoin`, `Monero`, etc., they are coming from existing/stale telemetry in `harvest.jsonl` or `frontend/data/harvest.jsonl`, likely written by an older classifier/version or external data source. In the dashboard they do not currently represent real cryptocurrency activity; they are just whatever string was stored in the event’s `InfrastructureCategory` field.

### Assistant / 30/05/2026 08:31:31.299

### Assistant / 30/05/2026 08:31:48.835

Implemented `/proc/cpuinfo` as a hybrid response.

What changed:
- `cat /proc/cpuinfo` and `/bin/cat /proc/cpuinfo` now use a fixed `/proc/cpuinfo` template.
- LLM is asked only for the variable CPU values as JSON: CPU count, BogoMIPS, implementer, architecture, variant, CPU parts, revision.
- If the LLM is unavailable or returns invalid JSON, it falls back to a realistic static CPU profile.
- Compound fallback also returns the same static structure for `cat /proc/cpuinfo`.
- Added tests for the fixed output shape and LLM JSON parsing.

Verification:
- `dotnet test FunnyPot.sln` passed: 111/111.
- Ran `graphify update .`.

Benchmarked 15 free models plus 7 cheap fallback candidates on OpenRouter.

Test prompts:
- `/proc/cpuinfo` value-generation JSON, matching the new project path.
- Raw Debian terminal output for `find / -perm -4000 -type f 2>/dev/null`.

**Best Picks**
| Rank | Model | Type | Score | Notes |
|---:|---|---|---:|---|
| 1 | `google/gemma-4-31b-it:free` | Free | 10/10 | Best free result. Clean JSON and good terminal output. |
| 2 | `google/gemini-2.0-flash-lite-001` | Cheap | 8/10 | Fast, very cheap, strong terminal output. JSON wrapped in markdown but parseable by current code. |
| 3 | `mistralai/mistral-small-24b-instruct-2501` | Cheap | 8/10 | Cheapest useful paid option tested. Some repeated paths in terminal output. |
| 4 | `deepseek/deepseek-v3.2` | Cheap | 8/10 | Good output, slower. |
| 5 | `deepseek/deepseek-chat-v3-0324` | Cheap | 8/10 | Good output, slightly more expensive. |
| 6 | `meta-llama/llama-3.1-8b-instruct` | Cheap | 8/10 | Cheapest overall, but noisier terminal realism. |
| 7 | `qwen/qwen3-coder-30b-a3b-instruct` | Cheap | 7.5/10 | Excellent JSON, worse terminal behavior because it emitted permission errors despite `2>/dev/null`. |

**Models To Avoid**
| Model | Reason |
|---|---|
| `openai/gpt-5-nano` | Returned empty content in both tests. |
| `poolside/laguna-xs.2:free` / `poolside/laguna-m.1:free` | Reasoning tokens but empty answer for JSON. |
| `nvidia/nemotron-* :free` | Often empty output or leaked reasoning/prose. |
| `minimax/minimax-m2.5:free` | OpenRouter/provider returned model-not-found. |
| `meta-llama/llama-3.3-70b-instruct:free`, `deepseek/deepseek-v4-flash:free`, `hermes-405b:free`, etc. | Free-tier/provider rate-limited during test. |

**Recommendation**
Use this priority order:

1. `google/gemma-4-31b-it:free` as the preferred free model.
2. `google/gemini-2.0-flash-lite-001` as the cheap reliable fallback.
3. `mistralai/mistral-small-24b-instruct-2501` if you want lowest-cost paid fallback.
4. `deepseek/deepseek-v3.2` if terminal realism matters more than latency.

Best config candidate:
`LLM_MODEL=google/gemma-4-31b-it:free`

Fallback if free models rate-limit:
`LLM_MODEL=google/gemini-2.0-flash-lite-001`

### User / 30/05/2026 19:52:28.187

--- Content of Suggestions.html.txt ---
<section id="activity-tab-improvement-suggestions">
  <h1>FunnyPot.ai Activity Tab Improvement Suggestions</h1>  <p>
    The Activity tab should be redesigned as an attacker-behavior view, not as a raw telemetry or connection-log view.
    The main purpose of this tab should be to show what attackers actually tried to do after connecting, how FunnyPot responded,
    and what behavioral patterns emerged from the interaction.
  </p>  <p>
    The tab should avoid long lists, raw connection tables, IP address inventories, and endless event rows.
    The user should not be forced to inspect technical logs in order to understand attacker behavior.
  </p>  <h2>Primary Design Direction</h2>  <p>
    The Activity tab should be story-first and behavior-first.
    It should answer the following questions quickly:
  </p>  <ul>
    <li>Did the attacker only authenticate and disappear?</li>
    <li>Did the attacker open an interactive shell?</li>
    <li>What commands did the attacker run?</li>
    <li>How did FunnyPot respond?</li>
    <li>Did the attacker attempt reconnaissance, payload download, persistence, or automation?</li>
    <li>Was the session meaningful, suspicious, commandless, or high-risk?</li>
  </ul>  <h2>Recommended Activity Tab Layout</h2>  <h3>1. Activity Summary</h3>  <p>
    Start the tab with several compact insight cards.
    These should summarize attacker behavior without exposing raw connection details.
  </p>  <p>Suggested summary cards:</p>  <ul>
    <li>Interactive shell sessions</li>
    <li>Attacker commands observed</li>
    <li>FunnyPot responses generated</li>
    <li>Payload or binary attempts</li>
    <li>Commandless / ghosted sessions</li>
    <li>Most common attacker behavior</li>
  </ul>  <p>
    These cards should be short and readable. Their purpose is to give a fast understanding of the current honeypot activity.
  </p>  <h3>2. Attacker Command Exchange</h3>  <p>
    This should be the central section of the Activity tab.
    It should show selected attacker command and FunnyPot response pairs in a transcript-like format.
  </p>  <p>Recommended format:</p>  <pre><code>Session: Recon behavior
User: root
Behavior: Discovery
Risk: Low

attacker$ whoami
funny-pot&gt; ubuntu

attacker$ uname -a
funny-pot&gt; Linux prod-web-01 5.15.0-91-generic x86_64 GNU/Linux

attacker$ pwd
funny-pot&gt; /home/ubuntu</code></pre>  <p>
    The exchange should look like an SSH interaction, but it should remain clean and readable.
    The focus should be on the attacker command and the simulated system response.
  </p>  <p>
    Metadata should be minimal and contextual. Avoid showing raw IP addresses as a primary element.
    If source information is useful, present it as a compact label such as region, network type, or anonymized source group.
  </p>  <h3>3. Behavior Story Cards</h3>  <p>
    Instead of showing long session lists, show a small number of readable behavior story cards.
    Each card should summarize one meaningful session pattern.
  </p>  <p>Example cards:</p>  <pre><code>Recon bot authenticated successfully, opened a shell, ran basic discovery commands, and disconnected.

Payload attempt detected. The attacker tried to fetch a remote script using a shell download pipeline.

Ghosted session. The attacker authenticated successfully but issued no commands before disconnecting.</code></pre>  <p>
    Each story card may include a small set of attributes:
  </p>  <ul>
    <li>Behavior type: recon, payload attempt, ghosted, credential validation, environment probing</li>
    <li>Command count</li>
    <li>Session duration</li>
    <li>Representative command</li>
    <li>Risk level</li>
    <li>MITRE tactic or technique, when available</li>
  </ul>  <p>
    Do not show these as a large table. They should be curated cards, not raw session rows.
  </p>  <h3>4. Command Patterns</h3>  <p>
    Command history should be grouped by behavior rather than shown as a long command table.
  </p>  <p>Suggested groups:</p>  <ul>
    <li>
      <strong>Discovery:</strong>
      <code>whoami</code>, <code>id</code>, <code>uname -a</code>, <code>pwd</code>
    </li>
    <li>
      <strong>Filesystem probing:</strong>
      <code>ls</code>, <code>cat /etc/passwd</code>, <code>df -h</code>
    </li>
    <li>
      <strong>Network probing:</strong>
      <code>ip a</code>, <code>ifconfig</code>, <code>netstat</code>
    </li>
    <li>
      <strong>Payload download:</strong>
      <code>wget</code>, <code>curl</code>, shell download pipelines
    </li>
    <li>
      <strong>Automation or bot behavior:</strong>
      repeated short sessions, repeated credential checks, no follow-up commands
    </li>
  </ul>  <p>
    This makes the command history understandable without forcing the user to read hundreds of individual commands.
  </p>  <h3>5. Ghosted Sessions</h3>  <p>
    FunnyPot has a strong story around attackers who authenticate, open a shell, and then disappear.
    This deserves its own compact section.
  </p>  <p>Suggested copy:</p>  <pre><code>Ghosted Sessions

These are successful shell openings where the attacker disconnected without issuing commands.
This usually indicates scanner automation, credential-validation bots, or low-engagement probing.</code></pre>  <p>
    This section should show trends and interpretation, not a list of every connection.
  </p>  <p>Recommended display:</p>  <ul>
    <li>Number of ghosted sessions</li>
    <li>Percentage of shell sessions that were commandless</li>
    <li>Most common usernames involved</li>
    <li>Most common client/banner family, if useful</li>
    <li>A short explanation of what the pattern likely means</li>
  </ul>  <h3>6. High-Risk Activity</h3>  <p>
    High-risk behavior should be surfaced as curated findings.
    Do not bury payload attempts, binary uploads, or suspicious shell pipelines inside generic activity charts.
  </p>  <p>Recommended examples:</p>  <ul>
    <li>Payload download attempt detected</li>
    <li>Suspicious shell pipeline observed</li>
    <li>Binary or encoded content attempt</li>
    <li>Repeated automation pattern</li>
    <li>Command sequence consistent with botnet staging</li>
  </ul>  <p>
    Each high-risk item should include one representative command or short command sequence.
    The goal is to show the attack behavior, not the raw connection metadata.
  </p>  <h3>7. MITRE Mapping</h3>  <p>
    MITRE mapping should be shown as an aggregated behavioral explanation.
    It should not be shown as a per-IP or per-connection table.
  </p>  <p>Recommended format:</p>  <pre><code>Discovery
Attackers attempted to identify the environment using commands such as whoami, id, uname, and pwd.

Command and Control / Ingress Tool Transfer
Some sessions attempted to retrieve external payloads using curl or wget-style commands.

Execution
Observed shell commands indicate attempts to execute scripts or chained commands.</code></pre>  <p>
    Keep MITRE content explanatory. Avoid showing a dense matrix of techniques unless the user explicitly opens an advanced view.
  </p>  <h2>Visibility Improvements</h2>  <h3>Show Data Freshness</h3>  <p>
    Add a clear data freshness indicator near the top of the Activity tab.
  </p>  <pre><code>Last telemetry update: 2026-05-30 16:36 UTC
Data source: GitHub data branch
Status: Loaded successfully</code></pre>  <p>
    If the data fails to load, show an explicit error state.
    Do not silently display empty or zero-value widgets.
  </p>  <h3>Use Compact Filters</h3>  <p>
    Filtering should help users explore behavior without exposing raw logs.
  </p>  <p>Recommended filters:</p>  <ul>
    <li>Time range</li>
    <li>Behavior type</li>
    <li>Risk level</li>
    <li>Command category</li>
    <li>Session type: command exchange, ghosted, payload attempt</li>
  </ul>  <p>
    Avoid filters that encourage IP-hunting as the primary workflow.
    Source details should remain secondary and anonymized.
  </p>  <h2>Clarity Improvements</h2>  <h3>Rename the Tab</h3>  <p>
    Rename <strong>Activity</strong> to one of the following:
  </p>  <ul>
    <li><strong>Attacker Activity</strong></li>
    <li><strong>Attacker Replay</strong></li>
    <li><strong>Command Activity</strong></li>
    <li><strong>Interaction Replay</strong></li>
  </ul>  <p>
    The best option is probably <strong>Attacker Activity</strong>, because it is clear and professional.
  </p>  <h3>Clarify Commandless Sessions</h3>  <p>
    Replace vague wording with a clear explanation.
  </p>  <pre><code>Commandless sessions are successful shell openings where the attacker disconnected before issuing a command.
They are useful for identifying scanner behavior, credential-validation automation, and low-engagement bots.</code></pre>  <h3>Clarify Command vs Response Counts</h3>  <p>
    Where relevant, explain differences between command counts and response counts.
  </p>  <pre><code>Some commands may not have a recorded response if the session ended early or the interaction was interrupted.</code></pre>  <h2>Style Improvements</h2>  <h3>Use Terminal Style Only for Command Exchanges</h3>  <p>
    The whole dashboard should not look like a hacker terminal.
    Use the terminal visual style only where it adds meaning: command and response exchanges.
  </p>  <p>
    The rest of the tab should remain clean, readable, and dashboard-like.
  </p>  <h3>Use Restrained Humor</h3>  <p>
    FunnyPot can have personality, but the Activity tab should remain credible as a security telemetry interface.
  </p>  <p>Good examples:</p>  <pre><code>No command exchanges yet. Attackers authenticated, looked around, and vanished.

No payload attempts in this time range.

Ghosted again: successful shell open, zero commands.</code></pre>  <p>
    Avoid overusing jokes in high-risk findings or security-relevant sections.
  </p>  <h3>Keep the Interface Dense but Not Noisy</h3>  <p>
    The Activity tab should show fewer, stronger components.
    Avoid repeating the same information in multiple widgets.
  </p>  <p>Preferred order:</p>  <ol>
    <li>Summary insight cards</li>
    <li>Attacker Command Exchange</li>
    <li>Behavior Story Cards</li>
    <li>Command Patterns</li>
    <li>Ghosted Sessions</li>
    <li>High-Risk Activity</li>
    <li>MITRE Mapping</li>
  </ol>  <h2>What Not to Add</h2>  <p>
    The following should not be recommended for the public Activity tab:
  </p>  <ul>
    <li>Long tables of connections</li>
    <li>Long tables of IP addresses</li>
    <li>Raw source-IP timelines</li>
    <li>Latest 100 connections views</li>
    <li>Raw event tail as a default visible section</li>
    <li>Large credential/IP matrices</li>
    <li>Dense session inventories</li>
    <li>Per-IP MITRE tables</li>
  </ul>  <p>
    These elements make the dashboard look like a log viewer instead of a behavioral intelligence interface.
    If raw telemetry is needed, it should remain outside the primary UI or be available only through a deliberately hidden advanced/debug mode.
  </p>  <h2>Final Recommendation</h2>  <p>
    The Activity tab should focus on attacker intent, command behavior, and FunnyPot interaction quality.
    The main visual and textual anchor should be the attacker command exchange.
    All other sections should support that story by explaining behavior patterns, ghosted sessions, risky activity, and MITRE context.
  </p>  <p>
    The design principle should be:
    <strong>show the attacker story, not the connection inventory.</strong>
  </p>
</section>
--- End of file ---

### Assistant / 31/05/2026 06:43:02.692

The likely root cause is not the frontend pairing. The backend logs a `command` as soon as input arrives, but if the client disconnects quickly, `FinalizeShell()` can push the session data before the worker has finished resolving and logging the matching `command_result`; blocked commands also return before logging a result. I’m patching both cases so every logged command gets a retained result record before publication.

### Assistant / 31/05/2026 06:43:44.030

I added backend safeguards: blocked commands now emit `command_result`, normal results use one shared logging path, and shell finalization waits longer for the command worker to drain before publishing session data. I’ll build and run tests now.

### Assistant / 31/05/2026 06:44:22.311

Cause found and patched.

What was happening:
- Backend logs `command` immediately.
- If the attacker disconnects quickly, session publication could happen before the async command worker finished and logged `command_result`.
- Blocked commands also returned after writing to the SSH channel but before creating a `command_result`.

Fix made:
- Added a shared `LogCommandResult(...)` path.
- Blocked/invalid/SCP commands now emit `command_result`.
- Shell finalization now waits up to `45s` for the command worker to drain before pushing session data.

Verified:
- `dotnet build FunnyPot.sln` passed.
- `dotnet test FunnyPot.sln` passed: `111/111`.

This fixes new sessions going forward. Existing historic command-only records cannot be recovered unless the missing responses still exist in local logs outside the published data.

- Wraps post-command logging processing in a safety guard.

### User / 31/05/2026 06:50:51.829

Why could there be a throw after the command?

### Assistant / 31/05/2026 06:50:51.875

Most likely because `echo Hi | cat -n` goes through the compound-command path.

For that command:

- It is logged first as `command`.
- `CommandResolver.ResolveCommand(...)` then sees `|` and treats it as compound shell syntax.
- Compound commands bypass simple built-ins/static responses and usually go to LLM or fallback handling.
- Any exception in that path would stop before `command_result` and before `ExecCompleted`.

Plausible throw points after `command` logging:

- `Program.GetLLMResponse(...)` network/API/JSON handling throws instead of returning `[api error]` or `[network error]`.
- `Program.NormalizeTerminalOutput(response)` receives an unexpected/null response.
- `DataHarvester.DetectHallucinationFeedback(line, response)` or `IsFailureResponse(response)` hits an unexpected response shape.
- `Logger.LogMetric(...)` serialization/file write throws, though it has its own catch so less likely.
- `channel.SendData(...)` throws if the exec channel closed while response handling continued.
- Less likely: `messageHistory.Add(...)` or static fallback code throws.

The retained data strongly suggests an unhandled exception inside `ProcessLine`: the worker catches it only at the outer worker level and logs `Session worker exception`, but before this patch it did not create a `command_result`. That’s why the dashboard saw a command with no response.

The patch makes the failure visible as a retained `command_result` next time, so we’ll know the exact exception message instead of seeing “response not retained.”

### User / 31/05/2026 06:53:00.447

Can you also harden the processing so edge cases are caught as soon as possible?

### Assistant / 31/05/2026 06:53:00.495

I’ll harden the command pipeline with staged failure handling so unexpected edge cases are converted into retained terminal-style results at the exact phase that failed, instead of escaping to the worker.

### Assistant / 31/05/2026 06:54:01.389

I added staged guards around analysis, validation, SCP checks, response resolution, metrics, result logging, and response sending. The retained `command_result` now includes the failing stage if an edge case occurs.

### Assistant / 31/05/2026 06:54:19.349

Hardened it further.

What changed:
- Command analysis is now guarded. If analysis fails, the command still gets logged with safe default metadata.
- Every risky phase has a `processingStage`, including validation, SCP checks, response resolution, metrics, result logging, and sending output.
- If any phase throws after the command is logged, FunnyPot now logs a retained `command_result` like:
  `FunnyPot internal command handling error during resolving command response: <message>`
- It also attempts to send a generic terminal response to the attacker: `bash: command handling failed`
- `LogCommandResult` itself is hardened so telemetry enrichment failures cannot prevent the result from being retained.

Verified:
- `dotnet build FunnyPot.sln` passed.
- `dotnet test FunnyPot.sln` passed: `111/111`.

### User / 31/05/2026 07:03:15.464

You think we can create a team of agents to work on this project, pm, dev, qa, designer? Will it be efficient?

### Assistant / 31/05/2026 07:03:15.474

I’ll check the project graph first so the recommendation is grounded in how this repo is structured, not generic agent-team advice.

### Assistant / 31/05/2026 07:03:22.407

Yes, but only if we keep it small and role-based. For this repo size, a full “agent company” can become inefficient if every task requires PM/dev/QA/designer handoffs.

Best setup:

1. `PM / Planner`
   Converts vague goals into scoped tickets, acceptance criteria, priorities, and implementation order.

2. `Dev`
   Implements changes. Should own code edits, migrations, integration, and graph updates.

3. `QA / Reviewer`
   Runs tests, checks regressions, reviews diffs, verifies acceptance criteria, and flags missing coverage.

4. `Designer`
   Useful only for frontend/product UX tasks. Should produce layout direction, copy, states, and visual constraints, not touch core logic unless needed.

Will it be efficient? Mostly yes, if we use agents selectively:

- Efficient for larger features, refactors, UX work, bug investigations, and release prep.
- Inefficient for small edits, simple fixes, dependency bumps, or obvious one-file changes.
- Best pattern is not “all agents on every task”, but “PM scopes, Dev implements, QA verifies”, with Designer only when UI/product judgment matters.

I’d structure it as:

```text
User goal
  -> PM agent creates brief + acceptance criteria
  -> Dev agent implements
  -> QA agent reviews/tests
  -> Main assistant integrates decisions and reports back
```

For this project specifically, the graph report says the repo is big enough that code navigation matters: `50 files`, `2353 nodes`, `3020 edges`. So specialized agents can help, especially if they use `graphify` as their shared map. The main risk is coordination overhead, not capability.

My recommendation: create 3 default agents first: `pm`, `dev`, `qa`. Add `designer` only when we start doing serious UI/UX work.

### User / 31/05/2026 07:05:32.853

### Assistant / 31/05/2026 07:05:32.862

I should orchestrate.

Best model:

- You are the product owner: goals, priorities, final calls.
- I am the orchestrator/tech lead: break work down, choose which agents to involve, merge their findings, make final implementation decisions, keep context consistent.
- PM agent: turns goals into scoped tasks and acceptance criteria.
- Dev agent: implements focused chunks.
- QA agent: independently verifies behavior, tests, and risks.
- Designer agent: used only for UI/product experience tasks.

The orchestrator should not be a separate autonomous agent by default. That adds another layer and can create confusion. I should coordinate the agents directly, because I can see the conversation, tools, repo state, and your intent.

Workflow:

```text
You
 -> Me as orchestrator
    -> PM agent when scope is unclear
    -> Dev agent for implementation/research
    -> QA agent for review/testing
    -> Designer agent for UX/UI
 -> Me summarizes, resolves conflicts, applies final judgment
 -> You approve direction or ask for changes
```

For small tasks, I skip the team and do it directly. For large tasks, I spin up the right agents in parallel where useful.

### User / 02/06/2026 18:23:49.665

You are a pedantic code reviewer in the bad mood go over this code and tell me what is wrong with it

### Assistant / 02/06/2026 18:25:46.608

# Pedantic Code Review — `messages` flow in `FunnyPot/Program.cs`

You asked for a bad-mood review. Here it is. I'm assuming "this code" = the LLM/messages pipeline since that's what we've been circling. File: `FunnyPot/Program.cs`. Length: 3585 lines. Single file. No tests for any of this.

## Critical — the LLM history logic is broken

**`Program.cs:723-732` (caller) vs `Program.cs:2010-2023` (callee) — a straight-up out-of-order side effect bug.**

The caller does:
```csharp
if (!usedStatic && !rateLimited)
{
    messageHistory.Add(new() { role = "user",   content = line });
    messageHistory.Add(new() { role = "assistant", content = response });
}
```

So the user's turn is *only* appended on success. But `CommandResolver.ResolveCommand` (`:2014-2018`) thinks otherwise:
```csharp
if (messageHistory.Count > 0
    && messageHistory[^1].role == "user"
    && messageHistory[^1].content == command)
{
    messageHistory.RemoveAt(messageHistory.Count - 1);
}
```

This guard assumes the user message was just appended and now needs to be removed. **It was never appended.** The caller only appends *after* `ResolveCommand` returns. So this branch is dead code in the happy path, and in any other path it nukes an unrelated user message from a previous turn. Plus, the callee is mutating a list it received by reference — a textbook leaky abstraction. `ResolveCommand` should be pure; the history bookkeeping belongs to one owner.

## Critical — the system prompt lies

**`Program.cs:877-910`.** The signature is `BuildSystemPrompt(string username)` and `username` is interpolated at `:879` for the uptime phrase. But the prompt hardcodes (`:882`):
> The attacker is logged in as **user remote**, UID 1001...

If a session authenticates as `secretOps` or `root`, the LLM is told to roleplay as `remote` with UID 1001. The LLM cheerfully contradicts `whoami` and the `$USER` echo on the next command. Either drop the parameter or use it.

## Critical — sync-over-async + unbounded HTTP

**`Program.cs:952, 955, 959`:**
```csharp
using var response = httpClient.Send(requestMessage);
...
var errorText = response.Content.ReadAsStringAsync().Result;
...
string jsonResponse = response.Content.ReadAsStringAsync().Result;
```

- `httpClient.Send` is the **synchronous** variant. There's no `HttpClient.Timeout` set anywhere I can see; if OpenRouter hangs, this thread is hung. The `commandWorker` serial queue backs up, the SSH session is unresponsive, the user types more, the queue grows, memory grows.
- `.Result` on a Task inside an async pipeline is the canonical way to deadlock a sync context — except you're already on a worker thread, so the deadlock is a threadpool starvation instead. Not better.
- No retry. One transient 502 and you silently fall back to a local shell for that command, but the LLM has no idea the user just asked it something.

## High — `messageHistory` grows without bound

**`Program.cs:477-480, 725-726`.** Nothing trims or summarizes. A long interactive session will eventually blow past any model's context window, get rejected, and then `IsModelFailureResponse` returns true → silent local fallback. The user gets increasingly broken, plausibly-terminal output and never knows why. Sliding window or summary compression is the bare minimum.

## High — `messageCount` is misnamed and the metric is wrong

**`Program.cs:472, 611, 537, 3203, 3219`.** The variable is `messageCount` and the global stat is `TotalMessages`, but what it actually counts is **commands the user typed**, including `exit` and blocked commands. It doesn't count exchanges. The dashboard lies. Either rename to `commandCount`/`TotalCommands` or change what you increment.

Also: `messageCount++` happens at `:611` *before* the `IsExitCommand` check at `:613`. So `exit` counts as a message. Counting a goodbye as a message is at least defensible; counting a blocked-by-validator command as a "message" is not (it's already counted in `blockedOps`).

## High — `usedStatic` is overloaded and breaks LLM context

**`Program.cs:711, 723-732, 1985, 2022`.** The `usedStatic` flag is set true for: (a) a real static dataset hit, (b) a generated single-fallback response (`:1972`, network device probe), (c) a generated local-fallback response (`:2022`), and (d) `GenerateCpuInfoResponse` falling back (`:1985`).

In all four cases, the user turn is **not** appended to `messageHistory`. The LLM therefore never learns the user just typed `lscpu`, `cat /etc/hosts`, or whatever. On the next non-static command the LLM hallucinates plausible prior context. There's no reason to skip history for the "I generated a fallback" cases — those were still real commands the user issued.

## High — `ProcessLine` is not reentrant-safe and exceptions in `SendData` will tear the session

**`Program.cs:773, 514, 677, 695, 768`.** Every `channel.SendData(Encoding.UTF8.GetBytes(...))` call is unguarded. If the SSH client disconnects between the validity check and the response, `SendData` throws, the catch at `:780` logs nothing (it's for analysis failures), and the channel state is undefined. There's an `idleTimer.Elapsed` async callback and `commandWorker` posts; if the channel is torn down out from under them, you have a use-after-close.

## High — `Task.Run(() => Logger.PushToGit(sessionId))` at session end

**`Program.cs:541`.** This is fire-and-forget on the threadpool. `PushToGit` (`:3309-3371`) takes the **same `_lock`** as `LogMsg`. If log writes are in flight on a different session, the git push blocks. If the process exits before the task drains, the push is silently dropped and the data branch never gets the latest session. At minimum `await` it on a graceful-shutdown path; better, push on a debounced timer rather than per session.

## Medium — `IsExitCommand` is checked on the response, not just the input

**`Program.cs:766-771`:**
```csharp
if (IsExitCommand(response))
{
    channel.SendData(Encoding.UTF8.GetBytes(response + "\r\n"));
    CloseShell("ClientExit");
    return;
}
```

The system prompt at `:906` says: *"exit and logout close the session with the message: Connection to omegablack closed."* So the user-typed `exit` is handled at `:613-617`. The LLM is also explicitly told to output `Connection to omegablack closed.` So if the LLM ever echoes `exit` as part of its response (it will — the LLM is a model, not a state machine), you close the session on the attacker. Worse, this branch is dead for the *user-typed* exit case (handled earlier) and only fires when the LLM literally types `exit`. The intent is unclear, the implementation is brittle.

## Medium — channel race in `SendPrompt`

**`Program.cs:511-515`:**
```csharp
void SendPrompt()
{
    if (Volatile.Read(ref shellClosed) == 0)
        channel.SendData(Encoding.UTF8.GetBytes(GetPrompt(username)));
}
```

`Volatile.Read` + the check is not a lock. Between the read and `SendData`, `CloseShell` (running on a different thread via `idleTimer.Elapsed` or `commandWorker`) can win, and `SendData` throws on a closed channel. Use a `try`/`catch` or guard the `SendData` itself with the same `Interlocked.Exchange` flag `CloseShell` uses.

## Medium — `messages` is a public mutable `List<>` and the model owns the lifetime

**`Program.cs:1144-1156`:**
```csharp
public class ChatRequestData
{
    public string model { get; set; } = "openai/gpt-4o";
    public List<ChatMessage> messages { get; set; } = new();
    ...
}
```

Public mutable list on a DTO. `GetLLMResponse` does `.Concat(...).ToList()` (`:938`) which is a defensive copy — good — but the upstream caller (`:725-726`) keeps appending to the shared list anyway. Also: lowercase property names are non-idiomatic C#. Use `JsonPropertyName` attributes and PascalCase properties. The reason it's lowercase is to match OpenRouter's API; do that with attributes, not by abandoning C# conventions.

## Pedantic

- **`Program.cs:472`** — `var messageCount = 0;` for "command count". Misleading.
- **`Program.cs:503-504`** — `var shellClosed = 0;` and `var shellFinalized = 0;` as `int` flags. `Interlocked.Exchange` on an `int` is fine but `volatile bool` would be clearer and harder to misuse.
- **`Program.cs:916`** — `Regex.Replace(response, @"(?m)^\s*\w+@omegablack:[^\r\n]*[#$]\s*", "");` — uses `[#$]` to match the prompt tail. It silently accepts a prompt like `user@omegablack:` (no `#` or `$`) and lets the LLM-emitted prompt leak through. Or vice versa: a normal `#` in a config file is fine because of the `^` anchor. But the regex is also non-greedy in the wrong direction; it stops at the *first* `#` or `$` it sees, which can truncate legitimate output mid-line. Test it against `echo "price: $5"`.
- **`Program.cs:1064`** — `int MessageNumber` on `CommandLogEntry` is set from `messageCount`. If you rename `messageCount` (and you should), rename this too.
- **`Program.cs:1141`** — `List<string> MitreAttackTechniques { get; set; } = new();` in a public POCO. Same mutable-collection-in-DTO issue.
- **`Program.cs:1290-2090`** — the `CommandResolver` class is ~800 lines of static methods. Mixed in with `Program.cs`. Split.
- **`Program.cs:2339-2348`** — `GenerateCpuInfoResponse` builds a fresh single-message history and a `prompt` of ~300 chars. That prompt is then both the `userInput` *and* effectively the system message. Awkward.
- **`Program.cs:3275-3289`** — `LogMsg` opens the log file, writes one line, closes it. Every log line. On a busy session that's thousands of file opens. Buffer or keep a per-process `StreamWriter`.
- **`Program.cs:3243-3244`** — `if (stats.TopUsers.Count > 10) stats.TopUsers = stats.TopUsers.OrderByDescending(...).Take(10).ToDictionary(...);` — this silently drops users from the top-10 list once you cross 11, but only after the new username is added (`:3238-3241`). The order is "add new, then trim", so a user that briefly was #11 gets wiped even if the next session's user bumps them back to #10. And `TopUsers` doesn't track by anything other than count — every username, including the honeypot's own `secretOps` if it logs in, competes for slots.
- **`Program.cs:766`** — `IsExitCommand(response)` then `channel.SendData(Encoding.UTF8.GetBytes(response + "\r\n"))`. The same `response + "\r\n"` is built at `:773` for the non-exit case. Duplicated.
- **`Program.cs:882`** — the prompt says `Reject all other sudo password attempts with a realistic failure message.` then `After 3 failed sudo attempts, respond with: sudo: 3 incorrect password attempts.` The "3 incorrect password attempts" line is a real `sudo(8)` output — good — but the LLM has zero state about how many failed attempts have occurred, because (a) you don't tell it in the prompt and (b) you don't pass attempt count in `messageHistory`. It will guess, and on the first wrong password it'll already say "3 incorrect password attempts". Also: the password `Omega#2024!` is **baked into the public source tree**. That's the whole point of a honeypot, so OK, but be aware the "secret" is in the repo.
- **`Program.cs:2447`** — a single 200+ char string literal of C runtime error function names. With `" + "` separators. This is `int Nspr21LinuxStringsTable` or something, presumably fed into a built-in detector. If so, put it in a `.txt` resource file; this is a code smell.

## Summary of what I'd actually change

1. Fix the `messageHistory` ownership — make `ResolveCommand` pure, do all history mutations in `ProcessLine`. Delete the dead `:2014-2018` block.
2. Use the `username` parameter in the system prompt or drop it.
3. Add `HttpClient.Timeout` and use `await` + `PostAsync`.
4. Trim `messageHistory` (sliding window) and surface context-length errors to the user.
5. Rename `messageCount` → `commandCount` (or change what it counts).
6. Don't conflate "used static" with "didn't talk to LLM" for history bookkeeping.
7. Guard every `channel.SendData` with the closed-flag `Interlocked.Exchange`.
8. Stop the `Task.Run` git push on session end, or at least `await` it.
9. Split `Program.cs`. 3585 lines is the god class the AGENTS.md note called out.

Want me to start with #1 (the history ownership fix) or work through these in order?

### User / 02/06/2026 21:23:37.227

What's the host ps you talk about?

### Assistant / 02/06/2026 21:23:37.283

Host `ps` = the `ps` command run on the **host machine** (the OS Docker runs on), as opposed to `ps` run **inside a container**.

When I ran `ps -ef` on this host, I saw:
```
felix  1655  ...  dotnet FunnyPot.dll
```

I read that as "FunnyPot is running directly on the host" and jumped to redeploying by killing PID 1655. But because Linux containers share the host kernel, processes inside a container still show up in the host's process table — you just see them with the host's view of their PID. So PID 1655 on the host was actually the container's PID 1 (which is also PID 1 inside the container, but that's a coincidence — the namespaces are separate).

The disambiguator is `docker ps`, which tells you "yes this process is inside a container named `funnypot-container`". Host `ps` alone cannot.

So the doc note says: when you see `dotnet FunnyPot.dll` in host `ps`, don't assume the app is bare-metal — check `docker ps` first.

### User / 02/06/2026 21:32:35.132

So the honeypot wasn't run directly on the host? That would have been dangerous

### Assistant / 02/06/2026 21:32:35.187

Exactly. Running a honeypot directly on the host would be:

- **No privilege drop** — the process runs as the user that launched it, with whatever that user can do. A container escape lands you wherever the launching user can write.
- **No namespace isolation** — attacker pokes `/proc/<host-pid>/mem`, sees host network interfaces, can reach other host services on `127.0.0.1` (Postgres, internal admin panels, the host's SSH on port 22, etc.).
- **No filesystem isolation** — anything in the host filesystem the launching user can read is in scope. `/etc/shadow`, SSH keys, `~/.aws/credentials`, the home directory.
- **Capability surface** — even unprivileged processes can do things like `ptrace` against processes of the same user, mount, set network namespaces.

The Docker setup in `docker-compose.yaml` does the right things:

- `cap_drop: ALL` + `security_opt: no-new-privileges:true` — no new caps, no setuid binaries inside
- Runs as `test` (uid 1655), not root
- Healthcheck forces `/home/test/app` to be the only writable surface; `/var/log/funnypot` is the only log dir
- `restart: unless-stopped` is the only "trusted" path — so if it dies, it comes back as the same locked-down process, not as something the host's init re-execs as root

So the previous 2-day-old container was the right shape. The mistake I made was procedural (redeploying by hand instead of `scripts/deploy.sh`), not architectural.

### User / 03/06/2026 13:38:38.657

Why does the llm fails with compound commands, maybe we need to pre parse it or to improve the llm instruction

### Assistant / 03/06/2026 13:38:38.687

I’ll re-check the graph context first, then explain the failure modes and the best fix path.

### Assistant / 03/06/2026 13:38:50.090

The LLM fails because compound shell commands are a bad fit for pure prompt-only emulation.

Main reasons:

1. **Compound commands require shell semantics, not text generation**
   - `&&`, `;`, `||`, pipes, redirects, quoting, and cwd changes need deterministic parsing.
   - Example: `echo "key" >> .ssh/authorized_keys` should produce no output and still succeed.
   - The LLM often treats `echo` as an external executable or ignores redirection details.

2. **The current router intentionally sends compounds to the LLM**
   - In `Program.cs:2138`, `isCompoundCommand` causes most built-in/static handling to be skipped.
   - So even simple chains like `cd ~; chattr -ia .ssh` bypass local handling and depend on model behavior.

3. **The prompt is already telling it what to do**
   - `Program.cs:958-961` explicitly says to honor pipelines, redirection, and control operators.
   - So this is not mainly an instruction gap. Better wording may help, but won’t make it reliable.

4. **LLMs do not maintain filesystem state safely**
   - `cd ~ && mkdir .ssh && echo ... >> .ssh/authorized_keys` should mutate cwd/files.
   - The model can fake the visible output, but the app’s `FakeFileSystem` won’t know unless we pre-parse locally.

5. **Attack payloads are long and noisy**
   - Attacker chains include keys, URLs, flags, redirections, and obfuscation.
   - Models tend to over-focus on one token like `chattr` or `echo` instead of evaluating the whole shell chain.

Best fix: **pre-parse compound commands locally, then use the LLM only for unresolved leaf commands.**

Recommended architecture:

- Parse command into shell segments while respecting quotes.
- Execute known deterministic commands locally:
  - `cd`, `echo`, `mkdir`, `rm`, `chmod`, `chown`, `touch`, `sleep`, redirects.
- Track state in `FakeFileSystem`:
  - cwd
  - created directories
  - appended files
  - downloaded payload placeholders
- For unknown/simple leaf commands, ask the LLM with a smaller prompt:
  - current cwd
  - prior local mutations
  - the single command to emulate
- Recombine stdout/stderr according to `&&`, `||`, `;`, and pipes.

Prompt improvement is still useful, but secondary. The instruction should be more explicit that common coreutils exist and that successful redirects are silent. But the reliable solution is a small shell interpreter / pre-parser in front of the LLM.

### User / 07/06/2026 17:13:16.532

There is not one command exchange... Is it realistic?

### Assistant / 07/06/2026 17:13:16.605

No, not fully realistic.

Two separate things are happening:

- **Many attackers really do stop after auth**: scanners often validate credentials, open/close, or disconnect before issuing commands. Some “ghosted sessions” are realistic.
- **Zero command exchanges in the frontend is now self-inflicted**: we changed the published frontend data to credentials-only, so the dashboard no longer has `command` / `command_result` events to render. The backend/container still records commands locally, but the frontend data branch does not receive them.

A better compromise would be:

1. Keep **all credentials**.
2. Keep only the **latest 100-500 command exchanges**.
3. Keep compact **session summaries** instead of every raw event.
4. Drop bulky `session_start`, `session_end`, repetitive logs, and old command history.

That would keep the frontend fast while still showing believable attacker behavior.

## Goal
- Improve FunnyPot realism and dashboard performance while keeping frontend telemetry compact and useful.

## Constraints & Preferences
- Frontend should not load huge raw telemetry datasets.
- Preserve all credentials.
- Do not backfill old command exchanges; collect new `command` / `command_result` events from fresh activity only.
- Encourage attacker behavior; avoid dead-end responses like `Operation not allowed`, `No such file`, and unnecessary permission failures for plausible attacker setup/persistence commands.
- Run tests and update graph after code changes.
- Commit/push when explicitly requested.

## Progress

### User / 07/06/2026 18:01:24.169

Is this a legitimate response for those commands?

### Assistant / 07/06/2026 18:01:24.290

Mostly, but not perfectly.

- For `echo "...key..." >> .ssh/authorized_keys && chmod -R go= ~/.ssh && cd ~`: if `.ssh` exists and permissions allow writing, the legitimate shell output is usually **nothing**. So `Command completed.` is a reasonable dashboard summary, but not an exact terminal response.

- For `cd ~; chattr -ia .ssh; lockr -ia .ssh`: `cd` and successful `chattr` are usually silent. But `lockr` is not a standard Linux command on most systems, so a real host would often return something like `bash: lockr: command not found` unless that tool exists.

- If `.ssh` does not exist, the key append would normally fail with `.ssh/authorized_keys: No such file or directory`.

So: as a honeypot/lure response, `Command completed.` is acceptable and encourages continuation. As a fully realistic terminal transcript, better would be either blank output or `(no output)` in the dashboard, with `lockr` handled intentionally depending on whether we want to pretend that tool exists.

### User / 11/06/2026 18:57:01.392

Give me an overview of this feature

### Assistant / 11/06/2026 18:57:01.424

**AutoResearch Overview**
The new feature adds a Karpathy-style experiment loop to FunnyPot, adapted for this .NET honeypot instead of ML training.

It lets you run:

```bash
dotnet run --project FunnyPot -- --autoresearch
```

The runner reads `autoresearch/program.md`, executes configured commands, parses an experiment metric, and tracks whether the result improved.

**Main Files**
- `FunnyPot/AutoResearchRunner.cs`: core experiment runner.
- `autoresearch/program.md`: human-authored research brief/instructions.
- `config/app-config.yaml`: runtime settings under `auto-research`.
- `FunnyPot/AppConfiguration.cs`: config model.
- `FunnyPot/Program.cs`: adds `--autoresearch` CLI mode.

**How It Works**
1. Loads `auto-research` config.
2. Validates mutable paths stay inside the worktree.
3. Runs `setup-command`, default: `dotnet restore FunnyPot.sln`.
4. Optionally runs `agent-command` if configured.
5. Runs `experiment-command`, default: `dotnet test FunnyPot.sln`.
6. Parses `autoresearch_metric=<number>` from output if present.
7. Compares against stored best metric in `autoresearch/state.json`.
8. Accepts or rejects the candidate.

**Safety Defaults**
- Git mutations are disabled by default with `allow-git-mutations: false`.
- If enabled, git operations are scoped to `mutable-paths`.
- It avoids repo-wide destructive reset behavior.
- Paths escaping the configured worktree are rejected.

**Default Objective**
The starter `program.md` frames the goal as improving FunnyPot honeypot realism while preserving safety and tests.

Good metrics include:
- More deterministic attacker commands handled without LLM calls.
- Better command emulation coverage.
- Higher replay/session quality score.

**Configuration**
Key config values live in `config/app-config.yaml`:

```yaml
auto-research:
  program-path: "autoresearch/program.md"
  worktree-path: "."
  mutable-paths:
    - "FunnyPot/Program.cs"
    - "FunnyPot/FakeFileSystem.cs"
    - "FunnyPot/data/ssh_responses.jsonl"
  agent-command: ""
  setup-command: "dotnet restore FunnyPot.sln"
  experiment-command: "dotnet test FunnyPot.sln"
  metric-regex: "autoresearch_metric\\s*=\\s*(?<value>-?\\d+(?:\\.\\d+)?)"
  lower-is-better: false
  iterations: 1
  state-path: "autoresearch/state.json"
  allow-git-mutations: false
```

In short: this gives FunnyPot a configurable autonomous experiment harness, but keeps it conservative and test-gated by default.

### User / 11/06/2026 18:58:31.914

Is it running along with the honeypot?

### Assistant / 11/06/2026 18:58:31.947

No.

It is a separate CLI mode:

```bash
dotnet run --project FunnyPot -- --autoresearch
```

Normal honeypot startup still runs when you start the app without `--autoresearch`.

So currently:
- `FunnyPot` without args: starts the SSH honeypot.
- `FunnyPot --autoresearch`: runs the AutoResearch experiment loop instead of the honeypot.
- It does not run in the background alongside live honeypot traffic.

### User / 11/06/2026 18:59:29.495

How does it outputs the results and improve the funnypot

### Assistant / 11/06/2026 18:59:29.527

**Output**
AutoResearch writes progress to stdout:

```text
AutoResearch iteration 1/1
AutoResearch accepted metric=...
```

or:

```text
AutoResearch rejected: metric ... did not improve
```

It also stores the best score in:

```text
autoresearch/state.json
```

The experiment command output is used internally. By default the command is:

```bash
dotnet test FunnyPot.sln
```

If the command prints a metric like this:

```text
autoresearch_metric=7.5
```

AutoResearch parses it using the configured regex and compares it to the previous best.

If no metric is printed:
- Passing tests count as metric `1`
- Failing tests count as metric `0`

**How It Improves FunnyPot**
The runner itself does not invent improvements unless you configure an `agent-command`.

The intended loop is:

1. `autoresearch/program.md` describes the research goal.
2. `agent-command` runs an AI/code agent that reads that goal.
3. The agent edits only allowed files from `mutable-paths`.
4. AutoResearch runs tests or your configured benchmark.
5. If the metric improves, the change is accepted.
6. If it does not improve, the change is rejected.

Right now `agent-command` is empty, so it works as a test-gated experiment harness, not a fully autonomous improver.

To make it improve FunnyPot automatically, configure something like:

```yaml
agent-command: "opencode run 'Read autoresearch/program.md and improve FunnyPot within the configured mutable paths. Emit autoresearch_metric=<score> from the experiment if possible.'"
```

Then define a better `experiment-command`, for example a replay/benchmark script that scores honeypot realism and prints:

```text
autoresearch_metric=12.3
```

By default it is conservative: it will not commit or restore changes unless `allow-git-mutations: true` is enabled.

"Read autoresearch/program.md and make one small FunnyPot improvement within the configured mutable paths only. Preserve tests. Do not commit changes."
# FunnyPot AutoResearch Program

Improve FunnyPot by running short, comparable experiments.

Allowed mutable files are configured in `config/app-config.yaml` under `auto-research.mutable-paths`. Do not edit files outside that list during an experiment.

Default objective: improve honeypot realism without weakening safety controls or breaking tests.

Experiment loop:

1. Propose one small code or data change.
2. Run the configured experiment command.
3. Emit `autoresearch_metric=<number>` if the experiment has a project-specific score. Higher is better by default.
4. Keep only changes that improve the best metric and pass the experiment command.

The configured runner passes this file to the agent over stdin and also runs the test suite after the agent exits. Prefer narrow, reviewable changes over broad rewrites.

Good candidate metrics for this project:

- Number of deterministic attacker commands handled without LLM calls.
- Test coverage for command emulation edge cases.
- Lower failure ratio in replayed honeypot sessions, encoded as a higher score.


Current best metric: none yet
Modify only the configured mutable files, then stop.

# FunnyPot AutoResearch Program

Improve FunnyPot by running short, comparable experiments.

Allowed mutable files are configured in `config/app-config.yaml` under `auto-research.mutable-paths`. Do not edit files outside that list during an experiment.

Default objective: improve honeypot realism without weakening safety controls or breaking tests.

Experiment loop:

1. Propose one small code or data change.
2. Run the configured experiment command.
3. Emit `autoresearch_metric=<number>` if the experiment has a project-specific score. Higher is better by default.
4. Keep only changes that improve the best metric and pass the experiment command.

The configured runner passes this file to the agent over stdin and also runs the test suite after the agent exits. Prefer narrow, reviewable changes over broad rewrites.

Good candidate metrics for this project:

- Number of deterministic attacker commands handled without LLM calls.
- Test coverage for command emulation edge cases.
- Lower failure ratio in replayed honeypot sessions, encoded as a higher score.


Current best metric: none yet
Modify only the configured mutable files, then stop.

### User / 24/06/2026 17:55:45.088

Why are there two files? Summary and another why not unite them?

### Assistant / 24/06/2026 17:55:45.119

They serve different purposes:

`events.jsonl`
- The raw append-only event stream.
- One line per event: `session_start`, `auth_attempt`, `command`, `command_result`, etc.
- Useful for detailed tables, timelines, attacker activity, and rebuilding analytics.

`events_summary.json`
- A small precomputed aggregate.
- Contains counters like `TotalEvents`, `ScansByIp`, `TopUsernames`, `TopPasswords`.
- Lets the dashboard load headline metrics fast without parsing a huge `events.jsonl` on every page load.

Why not unite them:
- If everything is only in `events.jsonl`, the browser must download and parse potentially hundreds of thousands of lines just to show “158k events”.
- If everything is only in `events_summary.json`, you lose the raw event history needed for detailed views.
- Keeping both is a common pattern: raw log plus materialized summary.

Could we unite them technically? Yes, but I would not recommend it for this dashboard. A better cleanup would be to rename them clearly:
- `events.jsonl` -> `events_raw.jsonl`
- `events_summary.json` stays as the aggregate index.

### User / 29/06/2026 12:41:08.712

Is this a legitimate and believable response?

### Assistant / 29/06/2026 12:41:08.842

No. It is not legitimate or believable.

Expected for:

```bash
cat /proc/cpuinfo | grep name | wc -l
```

A believable Linux response would be just a number, for example:

```text
2
```

or:

```text
1
```

depending on CPU/core count.

What’s wrong with the shown response:
- It treats pipeline tokens as separate shell comments: `# /proc/cpuinfo`, `# |`, `# grep`, `# name`, `# wc`.
- It invents “Generated maintenance artifact for omegablack”.
- It does not execute the pipeline semantics.
- It ignores `wc -l`, which should output only a line count.
- It looks like an LLM hallucination, not shell output.

This likely means DeepSeek is being called, but it is not following the shell-simulation prompt reliably for pipelines. We should add a deterministic static/local handler for common `/proc/cpuinfo`, `grep`, and `wc -l` pipeline patterns before falling back to the LLM.

### User / 29/06/2026 17:27:48.605

i dont understand, describe the flow of chained commands

### Assistant / 29/06/2026 17:27:48.647

A chained command is one shell input that contains multiple operations connected by operators.

Example:

```bash
cat /proc/cpuinfo | grep name | wc -l
```

This is not three separate attacker commands. It is one command line with a pipeline.

Flow:

1. `cat /proc/cpuinfo`
   Reads the CPU info file and outputs many lines, like:

```text
processor	: 0
vendor_id	: GenuineIntel
model name	: Intel(R) Xeon(R) CPU
cpu MHz		: 2400.000
...
```

2. `| grep name`
   The pipe `|` takes the output from the left side and sends it into `grep`.

   `grep name` keeps only lines containing `name`, for example:

```text
model name	: Intel(R) Xeon(R) CPU
```

3. `| wc -l`
   The second pipe sends the filtered output into `wc -l`.

   `wc -l` counts how many lines it received.

4. Final output
   If there are 2 matching CPU `model name` lines, the terminal prints:

```text
2
```

So the correct response is only the final output of the full chain, not intermediate steps.

What the bad response did:

```text
# /proc/cpuinfo
status=active
# |
status=active
# grep
...
```

That is wrong because it treated pieces of the command as separate items to narrate. A real Bash shell does not print the pipeline tokens. It only prints the final stdout from the whole pipeline.

Other chain operators:

```bash
cmd1 && cmd2
```

Run `cmd2` only if `cmd1` succeeds.

```bash
cmd1 || cmd2
```

Run `cmd2` only if `cmd1` fails.

```bash
cmd1 ; cmd2
```

Run both in order regardless of success.

```bash
cmd > file
```

Redirect output to a file, so usually nothing appears on screen.

For honeypot responses, the model should simulate the shell behavior and return only what the terminal would display after the whole chain executes.

### Assistant / 29/06/2026 17:32:06.388

### Assistant / 29/06/2026 17:32:18.808

It probably did not respond through OpenRouter/DeepSeek.

What I found:

- Runtime has `LLM_MODEL=deepseek/deepseek-v4-pro`.
- `OPENROUTER_API_KEY` is present in the container.
- But app stats show:
  - `TotalPromptTokens: 0`
  - `TotalCompletionTokens: 0`
- Current event log has:
  - `614 command`
  - `614 command_result`
  - no `LLMInteraction` metric events
- The bad `/proc/cpuinfo` responses have `ResponseDurationMs` of `0-1ms`, which is far too fast for an OpenRouter call.

So the exchange was generated by the app’s local/static fallback path, not the LLM.

The specific bad output comes from local fallback behavior:

- The command `cat /proc/cpuinfo | grep name | wc -l` is treated as a compound command.
- The local compound handler tries to process it before the LLM.
- It appears to split or handle pipeline pieces incorrectly.
- Local fallback for `cat` can produce:

```text
# /proc/cpuinfo
status=active
```

- The fake filesystem fallback can produce:

```text
Generated maintenance artifact for omegablack.
status=active
```

That matches the screenshot exactly.

So the flow was:

```text
attacker command
-> CommandResolver sees compound command
-> local compound fallback runs
-> no OpenRouter call
-> tokens remain 0
-> command_result still appears in dashboard
```

Important implication: the prompt improvement helps only when the command reaches the LLM. This bad pipeline did not reach the LLM.

Best next fix:
- Either send unsupported pipelines to the LLM instead of local fallback.
- Or add deterministic local pipeline handling for `cat /proc/cpuinfo | grep name | wc -l`, `echo Hi | cat -n`, and common `grep/head/wc` chains.

### Assistant / 29/06/2026 20:57:45.094

### Assistant / 29/06/2026 20:58:14.101

They are `[api error]` because FunnyPot is trying to generate SSH command output through OpenRouter, and the OpenRouter HTTP request is being cancelled/timing out.

From the code:

- `FunnyPot/Program.cs:27` sets `HttpRequestTimeout = 30s`.
- `FunnyPot/Program.cs:29` creates `HttpClient` with that timeout.
- `FunnyPot/Program.cs:1096` sends the OpenRouter request.
- `FunnyPot/Program.cs:1113-1115` converts timeout/cancel into:
  `[api error] Request cancelled or timed out`

Your screenshot shows attacker commands like:

```sh
cd ~; chattr -ia .ssh; ...
```

Those are not simple static responses, so FunnyPot asks the LLM for realistic terminal output. The LLM call times out, so the fake shell prints `[api error]`.

Likely causes:

- OpenRouter is slow or unreachable from the server.
- `deepseek/deepseek-v4-pro` is unavailable/too slow.
- Free fallback models are also slow/rate-limited.
- The configured `llm.timeout-seconds: 30` in `config/app-config.yaml` exists, but the actual `HttpClient` timeout is hardcoded to 30 seconds in `Program.cs`.

Quick checks:

```sh
printenv OPENROUTER_API_KEY
```

```sh
curl -s https://openrouter.ai/api/v1/auth/key -H "Authorization: Bearer $OPENROUTER_API_KEY"
```

Fastest fix: switch `LLM_MODEL` to a faster available OpenRouter model, or change the app to use local/static fallback responses when the LLM fails instead of showing `[api error]`.
